Conditions for Zakat Obligation: Ownership and Personal Status
States conditions for zakat: full ownership, free, adult, sane, Muslim; exempts mukatab and debtor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for zakat obligation in the Hanafi school and identify which individuals are exempt due to lack of full ownership.

Core Idea
Zakat requires full ownership (milk tamm) – any deficit in ownership blocks the obligation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah outlines the essential conditions that must be met before zakat becomes obligatory. It explains the concept of full ownership (milk tamm) and why certain individuals, such as a mukatab or a debtor under demand, are not liable. Understanding these conditions is critical for correct zakat calculation and for distinguishing between those who must pay and those who may receive zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wealth and its owner
The first condition is that the wealth must be owned. Unowned wealth (e.g., stolen, usurped) does not attract zakat.
2
Verify full ownership (milk tamm)
Full ownership requires both ownership of the corpus (raqabah) and the right of disposal (yad). A mukatab owns only yad, so no zakat.
Milk tamm is a unique Hanafi condition.
3
Check the owner's personal status
The owner must be free, adult (baligh), sane (aqil), and Muslim. Non-Muslims, children, and the insane are exempt.
The text mentions 'mukallaf' which combines adulthood and sanity.
4
Examine exemptions
A mukatab (slave under manumission contract) and a debtor who is being demanded payment are exempt from zakat on wealth equal to the debt.
The debtor's exemption applies to the amount of the debt; the surplus is zakatable.
5
Apply the debt subtraction rule
When calculating zakat, subtract the amount of debt due from total wealth. Zakat is paid on the remainder, provided it meets nisab and other conditions.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding deferred debts like a woman's dowry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مملوك ملكاً تاماً، على حرّ مكلف مسلم ، فلا تجب على مكاتب ومديون مطالب من جهة عبد بقدر دينه [0] مملوك ملكاً تاماً : أي رقبة ويد ( على حرّ مكلف) : أي عاقل، بالغ ، (مسلم، فلا تجب على مكاتب ) ؛ لعدم الملكِ النَّام ، فإن له ملك اليدِ لا ملكِ الرقبة ، ومديون) مطالب من جهة عبد بقدر دينه) دائماً يفيد ذكر هذا القيد في مصرف الزَّكاة فإنّه إذا كانت له كتب تبلغ النصاب وهو محتاج إليها يجوز له أخذ الزَّكاة وإلا لا، كذا في «النهاية». وأجيب عنه : بأن هذا القيد قائم مقام قوله : لا للتجارة»، كما أشار إليه في «غاية البيان» كقيد الركوب في الدواب والخدمة في العبيد. [١] قوله : مملوك ؛ فإن لم يكن مملوكاً فلا تجب فيه الزكاة، كالمال المغصوب والمسروق، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى أنها تجب إذا كان مملوكاً بسبب خبيث كعقد فاسد ونحوه، وينبغي أن يؤديها من مال طيب، وتفصيلُهُ في «الدر المختار»، وحواشيه. [٢] قوله : أي رقبة ويداً ؛ يعني يكون مملوكاً له ذاتاً وتصرفاً بحيث يقدر على التصرف فيه، وعلى الانتقالات الملكية فيه. (۳) قوله : أي عاقل ... الخ ؛ فلا تجب على كافر ومسلم مجنون وصبي. [٤] قوله : على مكاتب ؛ وهو العبد الذي قال له مولاه : إذ أديت إليَّ كذا مالاً فأنت حر، وهو عبد ما بقي عليه درهم، فالمالُ الذي يحصله لفك رقبته يملك فيه التصرف، وليس له ملك الرقبة ، فإنَّ كلَّ مال العبدِ مملوك للمولى كما تقرر في موضعه. [ 0 ] قوله : مطالب ؛ مثل المديون بثمن المبيع والأجرة والقرض وضمان الاستهلاك وغير ذلك، وهل يمنع دين مهر المرأة ؟ قيل : يمنع مؤجّلاً كان أو معجلاً ، وقيل : المؤجل لا يمنع ، وقيل : إن كان الزوج غير قاصد أداءه لا يمنع ؛ لأنه لا يعد ديناً في زعمه. كذا في «البناية» (٢). [٦] (٦) قوله : بقدر دينه ؛ فيزكّي الزائد عن مقدار دينه ؛ مثلاً إذا كانت أربعمئة درهم، وعليه دين مئتي درهم يزكي مئتي درهم.
Owned with full ownership, upon a free, accountable, Muslim. So it is not obligatory upon a mukatab and a debtor who is demanded by another to the extent of his debt. [0] 'Owned with full ownership' i.e., corpus and hand. 'Upon a free, accountable' i.e., sane, adult. 'Muslim, so it is not obligatory upon a mukatab' due to lack of growing ownership, for he has ownership of hand but not of corpus. 'And a debtor who is demanded by another to the extent of his debt' – this qualification is always mentioned regarding the disbursement of zakat, because if he has books reaching the nisab while he is in need of them, it is permissible for him to take zakat, otherwise not. So it is in al-Nihayah. And it was answered: that this qualification stands in place of his saying: 'not for trade,' as indicated in Ghayat al-Bayan, like the qualification of riding in animals and service in slaves. [1] His saying: 'owned' – if it is not owned, then zakat is not obligatory on it, like usurped and stolen wealth. And the absolute expression indicates that zakat is obligatory if it is owned through an evil cause like a void contract and similar, and he should pay it from good wealth, and its detail is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses. [2] His saying: 'i.e., corpus and hand' means that it is owned by him in essence and disposition, such that he is able to dispose of it and make transfers of ownership in it. [3] His saying: 'i.e., sane... etc.' – so it is not obligatory upon a disbeliever, an insane Muslim, and a child. [4] His saying: 'upon a mukatab' – he is the slave whose master says: if you pay me such and such wealth, you are free. He is a slave as long as one dirham remains upon him. So the wealth he earns to free his neck, he has the right to dispose of it, but he does not have ownership of the corpus, for all of the slave's wealth is owned by the master as established in its place. [5] His saying: 'demanded' – like the debtor for the price of a sold item, rent, loan, guarantee of consumption, and other things. Does the debt of a woman's dowry prevent? It is said: it prevents whether deferred or immediate. And it is said: the deferred does not prevent. And it is said: if the husband does not intend to pay it, it does not prevent, because it is not considered a debt in his view. So it is in al-Binayah. [6] His saying: 'to the extent of his debt' – so he pays zakat on the surplus above his debt. For example, if he has four hundred dirhams and owes two hundred dirhams, he pays zakat on two hundred dirhams.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement specifying the conditions for the obligation of zakat. It states that zakat is obligatory on wealth that is: 1) owned with full ownership (both corpus and usufruct), 2) owned by a free person, 3) owned by a person who is mukallaf (sane and adult), and 4) owned by a Muslim. Therefore, zakat is not obligatory on: a mukatab (a slave under contract of manumission), because he does not have full ownership (he has only hand ownership, not corpus ownership; his earnings are ultimately owned by the master). Also, a debtor who is being demanded payment is exempt from zakat on wealth equal to the amount of his debt (the debt prevents zakat to that extent). The commentator explains that 'full ownership' means ownership of both the substance (raqabah) and the right of disposal (yad). Being 'mukallaf' excludes the insane and children, and being Muslim excludes non-Muslims. The passage also addresses an important point: if wealth is owned through an evil means (like a void contract), zakat is still obligatory, but the person should pay it from pure wealth (meaning he should repent and give zakat from lawful money). Regarding the debtor, the qualification 'demanded' is similar to the condition that the asset is not for trade; just as books needed for a scholar exempt him from zakat, debt to the extent of nisab exempts the debtor from zakat. There is some ikhtilaf concerning a woman's dowry debt: some say it prevents zakat, others say only if it is immediate, and some say if the husband does not intend to pay, it does not prevent. The amount of debt is subtracted from the total wealth, and zakat is paid on the remainder, provided the remainder is still above nisab and meets other conditions.

Key Terms
ملك تام (milk tāmm): Full ownership encompassing both the corpus (raqabah) and the right of disposal (yad), allowing the owner to freely transact.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission with his master, to be freed upon paying a specified amount. He has ownership of hand but not corpus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is zakat not obligatory on a mukatab according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because he is a slave and slaves are not subject to zakat at all
B) Because he lacks ownership of the corpus (raqabah) of his wealth
C) Because he is not a Muslim
D) Because his wealth is less than nisab

Key Takeaway
Zakat is only obligatory on wealth that is fully owned (milk tamm) by a free, adult, sane Muslim; debts and lack of full ownership lift the obligation.

Debt to Allah Does Not Block Zakat
Ruling with proof (ta'lil) on zakat condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between debts that block zakat obligation and debts that do not, explain the Hanafi reasoning (ta'lil) for each category, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving vows, expiations, and zakat debts.

Core Idea
Debt to Allah does not block zakat; only debts with human claimants do.

In this lesson, we examine the principle that debt only prevents zakat when a human can demand repayment. Debts owed solely to Allah — such as expiations or vows — do not remove the obligation. However, a zakat debt itself is considered a human debt due to the imam's claim, thus it does prevent. The passage also clarifies an intra-Hanafi dispute about the zakat debt's effect after property is consumed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The passage states that the condition for zakat is that the property is free from debt claimed by people (dain al-ibad). Debts owed only to Allah (dain Allah) do not prevent zakat.
The key distinction is the presence of a human claimant.
2
Recognize the exception
Examples of dain Allah include vows (nazr), expiations (kaffarah), sadaqat al-fitr, and udhiyah. These do not block zakat because no human can demand payment.
Even if the property is partially encumbered by such debts, zakat is due on the entire nisab.
3
Understand the mis-example
The text initially lists zakat itself as an example of dain Allah but then corrects this. Zakat debt has a human claimant — the imam or his deputy — so it is dain al-ibad and does prevent zakat.
This correction is crucial for correct application.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Zufar held that zakat debt never prevents. A narration from Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the zakat debt remains on tangible property ('ayn) it prevents; if it is only a debt in the dhimmah (after consumption) it does not. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree in the zahir al-riwayah that zakat debt always prevents.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the three imams.
5
Apply to a scenario
For a man who had 1000 dinars, consumed them after the year without paying zakat, then later obtained 200 dirhams: according to the three imams, zakat is not due on the 200 because the previous zakat is a debt in his dhimmah with a human claimant. According to Abu Yusuf's narration, zakat is due.
This illustrates the practical consequence of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن ملكه غير فاضل عن الحاجة الأصلية، وهي قضاء الدين، وإنما قيد بكونه مطالباً من عبد حتى لو كان مطالباً من الله لا يمنع وجوب الزكاة، كمن ملك نصاباً بعضُه مشغول بدين الله النذر، أو الكفارة، أو الزكاة تجب فيه الزكاة، ولا يشترط لوجوب الزكاة فراغُهُ عن هذا الدين. وقوله: بقدر دينِهِ، متعلّق بقولِهِ: فلا تَجِب: أي لا تَجِبُ على المديون بقدر ما يكونُ ماله مشغولاً بالدين. قوله: كالنذر؛ كما إذا كانت له مائتا درهم، ونذر أن يتصدق بمئة منها، فإذا حال عليهما تلزمه زكاتُها، ويسقط النذر بقدر درهمين ونصف؛ لأنه استحق لجهة الزكاة، فيبطل النذر فيه، ويتصدَّقُ بباقي المئة، ولو تصدق بكلها للنَّذرِ وقع عن الزَّكاة درهمان ونصف لتعينه بتعيين الله. كذا في «معراج الدراية». قوله: أو الكفارة؛ أي بأنواعها؛ ككفارة اليمين، وكفارة الظهار، وكفارة فطر صوم رمضان، وغير ذلك، وكذا صدقة الفطر، وهدي المتعة، والأضحية، فهذه الديون الواجبة في الذمة التي ليس لها مطالب من جهة العباد لا تمنع وجوب الزكاة. قوله: أو الزكاة؛ التمثيل بهذا خطأ نبه عليه الشارح الهروي، وابن كمال باشا في «الإيضاح والإصلاح» وغيرهما، إلا أن يحمل ذلك على صورة بقاء دين الزكاة باستهلاك المال على رأي أبي يوسف له لكنه رواية غير معتبرة عندهم. وتفصيله على ما في «الهداية» وشروحها، و«المحيط» وغيرها: أن دين الزكاة سواء كان في الأموال الظاهرة أو الباطنة يمنعُ وجوب الزَّكاة؛ لأنَّ له مطالباً من جهة العباد، وهو الإمامُ أو نائبه، وهذا عند علمائنا الثلاثة في ظاهر الرواية. نعم؛ خالف فيه زفر له وقال بعدم المنع مطلقا، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه إن كان في العين يمنع وإن كان في الذمة لا يمنع. وصورته: رجلٌ له ألف دينار، فاستهلكه بعد الحول قبل أداء الزكاة، ثم حصلت عنده مائتا درهم، لا تجب فيه الزكاة؛ لأن زكاة النصاب الأول دين في ذمته لسبب الاستهلاك، هذا عندهم، وعند أبي يوسف على رواية عنه: تجب فيه، ولا يمنع هذا الدين.
Because his property is not surplus beyond the essential need, which is repayment of debt. It is only restricted to being demanded by a servant [human], such that even if it is demanded by Allah, it does not prevent the obligation of zakat. For example, if one owns a nisab part of which is occupied by a debt to Allah – a vow, or expiation, or zakat – zakat is obligatory on it, and freedom from this debt is not a condition for the obligation of zakat. And his statement: 'to the extent of his debt' is attached to his statement: 'it is not obligatory', meaning: it is not obligatory on the debtor to the extent that his property is occupied by the debt. His statement: 'like a vow'; as if he has two hundred dirhams and vows to give charity with one hundred of them, then when a year passes on both, its zakat becomes obligatory on him, and the vow falls away to the extent of two and a half dirhams, because it has become due for the sake of zakat, so the vow is void in that amount, and he gives charity with the remaining one hundred. If he gave all of it for the vow, two and a half dirhams would count for zakat because of its specification by Allah's specification. Thus in 'Miraj al-Diraya'. His statement: 'or expiation'; meaning its types; like expiation for an oath, expiation for zihar, expiation for breaking the fast of Ramadan, and other such things. Likewise, sadaqat al-fitr, the hadi of tamattu', and the udhiyah. These debts obligatory in the dhimmah that have no claimant from the side of people do not prevent the obligation of zakat. His statement: 'or zakat'; representing this is an error, pointed out by the commentator al-Harawi and Ibn Kamal Pasha in 'al-Idah wa al-Islah' and others, unless it is taken to mean the case of the survival of the debt of zakat through the consumption of the property according to the opinion of Abu Yusuf, but it is a non-considered narration according to them. The detail according to what is in 'al-Hidayah' and its commentaries, and 'al-Muhit' and others: that the debt of zakat, whether on apparent or hidden wealth, prevents the obligation of zakat because it has a claimant from the side of people, which is the imam or his deputy. This is according to our three imams in the apparent transmission. Yes, Zufar differed with him and said it does not prevent at all, and from Abu Yusuf: that if it is in 'ayn (tangible property) it prevents, and if it is in the dhimmah it does not prevent. Its example: a man has one thousand dinars, so he consumes it after the year before paying zakat, then he obtains two hundred dirhams; zakat is not obligatory on it because the zakat of the first nisab is a debt in his dhimmah due to the consumption. This is according to them, and according to Abu Yusuf in one narration: zakat is obligatory on it, and this debt does not prevent.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that debt prevents the obligation of zakat. The author explains that the condition for zakat is that one's property exceeds one's essential needs, which includes repayment of debts to people. However, debts owed to Allah (such as a vow, expiation, or even zakat itself according to an initial mis-example) do not have a human claimant, so they do not prevent zakat. The author then corrects the mis-example of zakat: zakat debt actually has a human claimant (the imam or his deputy), so it does prevent zakat on other property. This is the view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad in the zahir al-riwayah. Zufar disagreed, saying zakat debt does not prevent at all. There is also a narration from Abu Yusuf distinguishing between zakat debt on tangible property (which prevents) and debt in the dhimmah (which does not). The practical example clarifies: if someone had a full nisab at year-end, consumed it before paying zakat, then later acquired 200 dirhams, according to the three imams, zakat is not due on the 200 dirhams because the previous zakat remains a debt. According to Abu Yusuf's narration, zakat is due. The relied-upon position is that of the three imams, as it is the basis of fatwa.

Key Terms
دين الله (dain Allah): A debt owed to Allah, such as nazr (vow), kaffarah (expiation), etc., which has no human claimant.
دين العباد (dain al-ibad): A debt owed to a human being, which has a claimant from the side of people.
زكاة الدين (zakāt al-dain): The zakat that was due on a previous nisab but not paid; it becomes a debt in the dhimmah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the text, which of the following is considered a 'dain al-ibad' (debt to people) that prevents zakat obligation?
A) Nazr to give charity
B) Kaffarah for breaking an oath
C) Zakat debt (when the property was consumed after the year)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Only debts owed to people block zakat; debts to Allah do not, but a zakat debt itself counts as a human debt.

Mal Dhimar: Six Categories Exempt from Zakat for Past Years
Listing types of effectively lost property exempt from past zakat upon recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six categories of mal dhimar and apply the ruling that no zakat is due for past years upon recovery.

Core Idea
Property effectively nonexistent in the owner's hand requires no zakat for the missing years.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh text clarifies when property recovered after a period of inaccessibility does not require zakat for the past years. The key is whether the owner had effective access; if the property was truly lost or inaccessible (mal dhimar), zakat is not due. The text lists six specific situations, with important nuances such as the exception for grazing livestock and the distinction between burial in a desert versus a garden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Understand the concept of 'mal dhimar' – property that is legally considered nonexistent because it is inaccessible to the owner. This principle exempts the owner from zakat for the years of inaccessibility upon recovery.
2
List the six categories
Enumerate from the text: 1) Lost property found later; 2) Property fallen into the sea and recovered later; 3) Usurped property for which the owner has no evidence; 4) Money buried in a desert and forgotten, then found; 5) A debt denied by the debtor for years, later acknowledged; 6) Property taken by confiscation (musadara) and later returned.
3
Note conditions and exceptions
For each category, identify the condition that qualifies it as dhimar. For example, usurped property requires no evidence; burial in a house or garden does not qualify; for usurped grazing livestock (sa'imah), zakat is not due even if evidence exists because the condition of sa'imah is broken.
Pay attention to the exception for sa'imah in the case of usurpation.
4
Distinguish from non-dhimar cases
Contrast each category with a similar case where zakat for past years is due: e.g., property lost in the house (not dhimar), burial in a garden (not dhimar), debt with a witness (zakat due upon recovery).
The decisive factor is the owner's access or ability to prove the property.
5
Apply to a novel scenario
Students should practice by analyzing a scenario: e.g., a man's money was confiscated by an unjust ruler ten years ago and returned today. Apply the ruling that no zakat is due for the past years. Then modify: what if the same money was merely mislaid in his own house? Then zakat is due.
Ensure the student checks both the existence of dhimar and the specific category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في مال مفقود، وساقط في بحر، ومغصوب لا بينة عليه، ومدفون في بَريَّةٍ نُسِي مكانه، ودين جحده المديون سنين ثُمَّ أقر بعدها عند قوم، وما أُخِذَ مصادرة ووصل إليه بعد سنين. هذه الأمثلة أمثلة المال الضمار. قوله: ولا في مال مفقود؛ أي لا تجب زكاة مال فُقِدَ عدة سنين ثم وجده، فلا تجب عليه زكاة السنين الماضية؛ لأنه كالمعدوم حكماً. قوله: وساقط في بحر؛ أي لا تجب الزَّكاةُ في مال سقط في بحر، واستخرجه بعد سنين. قوله: ومغصوب؛ أي لا تجب زكاة مال غصبه رجل، ولا بينة للمالك على غصبه حتى يتيسر له إثباته وأخذه منه، فإذا وجدَهُ بعد سنين لا يجب أداء زكاة الماضي، أما إذا كانت له بينة تجب عليه زكاة ما مضى بعد قبضه من الغاصب، إلا في غصب السائمة فلا تجب، وإن كان الغاصبُ مقراً لعدم تحقق الإسامة. كذا في «الخانية». قوله: في بَريَّة؛ بفتح الباء الموحدة وتشديد الياء المثناة التحتية: أي مفازة، فإذا دفن مالاً في صحراء ونسي مكانَه، ثمَّ تذكرَهُ بعد سنين واستخرجه لا تجب زكاة الماضي بخلاف المدفون في بيت أو بستان ونحو ذلك، فإنّه تجب فيه الزكاة؛ لأنه ليس بضمار. كذا في «البناية». قوله: ودين؛ أي إذا كان للرجل على آخر دين بقدر النصاب فأنكر المديون كونه عليه، ولا بيَّنة للمالك عليه، ثمّ أقرَّ المديون بعد مدَّةٍ عند قوم أنه عليه لا تجب عليه زكاة زمان الحجود. قوله: مصادرة؛ أي من حيث المصادرة، وهو ما يأخذه السلطان من رعيته من غير حق، والفرق بينه وبين الغصب: أنَّ الغصب أخذ المال مباشرة قهراً، والمصادرة أن يأمره بأن يأتي كذا من المال، فما أخذه السلطان ونحوه مصادرة، ثم ردَّهُ إليه بعد سنين، لا تجب عليه زكاة ما مضى.
And not on lost property, and property that fell into the sea, and usurped property for which there is no evidence, and property buried in a desert whose place was forgotten, and a debt denied by the debtor for years after which he acknowledged it before some people, and property taken by confiscation and which reaches him after years. These examples are examples of mal dhimar (lost property). His statement: 'and not on lost property'; meaning: zakat is not obligatory on property that was lost for several years and then he finds it, so zakat for the past years is not obligatory on him, because it is considered nonexistent legally. His statement: 'and property that fell into the sea'; meaning: zakat is not obligatory on property that fell into the sea and he extracted it after years. His statement: 'and usurped property'; meaning: zakat is not obligatory on property usurped by a man, and the owner has no evidence for the usurpation to enable him to prove it and take it from him; then if he finds it after years, paying zakat for the past is not obligatory. If he has evidence, then after receiving it from the usurper, zakat for the past is obligatory, except in the case of usurpation of grazing livestock (sa'imah), then it is not obligatory, even if the usurper admits, because the condition of sa'imah is not realized. Thus in 'al-Khaniyya'. His statement: 'in a desert'; with fatha on the unified ba' and tashdid on the ya' of the dotted two below: meaning a desert; if he buries property in a desert and forgets its place, then remembers it after years and extracts it, zakat for the past is not obligatory, contrary to what is buried in a house or garden or the like, for then zakat is obligatory on it because it is not dhimar. Thus in 'al-Binaya'. His statement: 'and a debt'; meaning: if a man has a debt on another equivalent to nisab, and the debtor denies it to be on him, and the creditor has no evidence on him, then after a period the debtor acknowledges before some people that it is on him, zakat for the time of denial is not obligatory. His statement: 'confiscation'; meaning: from the viewpoint of confiscation, which is what the ruler takes from his subjects without right. The difference between it and usurpation is that usurpation is taking property directly by force, while confiscation is that he orders him to bring such and such property. So what the ruler or the like takes by confiscation, then returns it to him after years, zakat for the past is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This commentary lists six categories of 'mal dhimar' (property effectively considered nonexistent) for which no zakat is due for the years the owner lacked access. The categories are: 1) Lost property found after years; 2) Property that fell into the sea and later recovered; 3) Usurped property with no evidence (if evidence exists, zakat is due upon recovery, except for grazing livestock); 4) Money buried in a desert that was forgotten and later found (if buried in a house or garden, zakat is due because it is not considered dhimar); 5) A debt that was denied by the debtor for years and then acknowledged (zakat not due for period of denial); 6) Property taken by confiscation (sadara) from a ruler and later returned. In all these cases, the property was effectively nonexistent from the owner's perspective during the missing period, so no zakat is owed for those past years. The exception for grazing livestock (sa'imah) is because even if the usurper admits, the condition of the animal being free-ranging (sa'imah) is not met while in usurpation.

Key Terms
المال الضمار (al-māl al-ḍimār): Property that is effectively lost or inaccessible, such that it is considered nonexistent legally; no zakat is due for past years upon recovery.
بَرّيّة (barriyyah): A desert or wilderness; property buried there and forgotten is considered dhimar.
مصادرة (muṣādara): Confiscation; property taken by a ruler without right, distinguished from usurpation (ghasb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a category of mal dhimar according to the Hanafi text?
A) Property that fell into the sea and was recovered after years
B) Property buried in a house and forgotten for years
C) A debt denied by the debtor for years and later acknowledged
D) Property taken by confiscation and later returned

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, property that is effectively nonexistent (mal dhimar) does not incur zakat for past years upon recovery, with specific categories defined by conditions of inaccessibility.

Definition and Ruling of Mal al-Dhimar in Zakat
Defines mal al-dhimar with examples and states the Hanafi Zakat ruling with proofs and Shafi'i comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mal al-dhimar, list its examples, state the Hanafi ruling that no Zakat is due on it, and explain the condition of effective possession (yad) as the basis for the ruling.

Core Idea
Zakat is not due on wealth that is inaccessible (mal al-dhimar) because effective possession (yad) is a condition for obligation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya defines the category of mal al-dhimar (inaccessible wealth) and clarifies its Zakat ruling according to the Hanafi school. Understanding this concept is crucial for correctly determining Zakat obligations on wealth that is lost, stolen, or otherwise out of one's control. The ruling is built on the principle that Zakat requires both legal ownership and effective possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept and its examples
The passage defines mal al-dhimar and gives six examples. List them: lost property, sunken property, usurped property without evidence, buried property in forgotten desert, debt denied for years, confiscated property. Understand that all share the feature of being inaccessible.
Some examples have exceptions (e.g., usurped with bayyinah, buried in house).
2
State the Hanafi ruling and condition
The Hanafi ruling is that Zakat is not due on mal al-dhimar for past years. The condition is that effective possession (yad) is required for Zakat obligation; dhimar lacks yad.
This differs from mere legal ownership (raqaba).
3
Analyze the proof
The author cites a narration from Hasan al-Basri and the practice of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. Understand the chain and the specific wording that excludes dhimar.
The hadith is mursal (zanni thubut), but it is supported by usul.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school does not require yad; thus they would obligate Zakat on dhimar for past years. Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة شروط الزكاة
ولا في مال مفقود، وساقط في بحر، ومغصوب لا بينة عليه، ومدفون في بَرية نُسِي مكانه، ودين جحده المديون سنين ثم أقر بعدها عند قوم، وما أُخِذَ مصادرة ووصل إليه بعد سنين. هذه الأمثلة أمثلة المال الضمار.

(١) قوله: ولا في مال مفقود؛ أي لا تجب زكاة مال فُقِدَ عدة سنين ثم وجده، فلا تجب عليه زكاة السنين الماضية؛ لأنه كالمعدوم حكماً. (٢) قوله: وساقط في بحر؛ أي لا تجب الزكاة في مال سقط في بحر، واستخرجه بعد سنين. (٣) قوله: ومغصوب؛ أي لا تجب زكاة مال غصبه رجل، ولا بينة للمالك على غصبه حتى يتيسر له إثباته وأخذه منه، فإذا وجده بعد سنين لا يجب أداء زكاة الماضي، أما إذا كانت له بينة تجب عليه زكاة ما مضى بعد قبضه من الغاصب، إلا في غصب السائمة فلا تجب، وإن كان الغاصب مقراً لعدم تحقق الإسامة. كذا في «الخانية». (٤) قوله: في برية؛ بفتح الباء الموحدة وتشديد الياء المثناة التحتية: أي مفازة، فإذا دفن مالاً في صحراء ونسي مكانه، ثم تذكره بعد سنين واستخرجه لا تجب زكاة الماضي بخلاف المدفون في بيت أو بستان ونحو ذلك، فإنه تجب فيه الزكاة؛ لأنه ليس بضمار. كذا في «البناية». (٥) قوله: ودين؛ أي إذا كان للرجل على آخر دين بقدر النصاب فأنكر المديون كونه عليه، ولا بينة للمالك عليه، ثم أقر المديون بعد مدة عند قوم أنه عليه لا تجب عليه زكاة زمان الحجود. (٦) قوله: مصادرة؛ أي من حيث المصادرة، وهو ما يأخذه السلطان من رعيته من غير حق، والفرق بينه وبين الغصب: أن الغصب أخذ المال مباشرة قهراً، والمصادرة أن يأمره بأن يأتي كذا من المال، فما أخذه السلطان ونحوه مصادرة، ثم رده إليه بعد سنين، لا تجب عليه زكاة ما مضى.

عندنا لا تجب الزكاة في المال الضمار، خلافاً للشافعي؛ بناء على أن لا يشترط لوجوب الزكاة كون النصاب مملوكاً رقبة ويداً، والمال الضمار بأقسامه ليس بمملوك يداً وإن كان مملوكاً رقبة. ويؤيده ما أخرجه أبو عبيد في «كتاب الأموال» بسنده عن الحسن البصري قال: «إذا حضر الوقت الذي يؤدي فيه الرجل زكاته أدى عن كل مال، وعن كل دين إلا ما كان منه ضماراً لا يرجوه». وفي «موطأ مالك» عن أيوب: «إن عمر بن عبد العزيز كتب في مال قبضه بعض الولاة ظلماً يأمر برده إلى أهله، ويؤخذ زكاته لما مضى من السنين، ثم عقب بعد ذلك بكتاب أن لا يؤخذ منه إلا زكاة سنة واحدة، فإنه كان ضماراً». وفي «مصنف ابن أبي شيبة»: عن عمر بن ميمون، قال: «أخذ الوليد بن عبد الملك عن رجل من أهل الرقة يقال له أبو عائشة عشرين ألفاً فألقاه في بيت المال، فلما ولي عمر بن عبد العزيز أتاه ولده فرفعوا مظلمتهم إليه، فكتب إلى ميمون أن ادفعوا إليهم أموالهم، وخذوا زكاة عامهم هذا، فإنه لولا أن كان مالاً ضماراً أخذنا منه زكاة ما مضى». كذا في «تخريج أحاديث الهداية» للزيلعي.
The Book of Zakat: Conditions of Zakat. And (no Zakat is due) on lost property, and (property) fallen into the sea, and usurped property for which there is no evidence, and (property) buried in a desert whose place is forgotten, and a debt that the debtor denied for years then later admitted before people, and what was taken by confiscation and reached him after years. These examples are examples of mal al-dhimar. (1) His statement: 'And not on lost property'; meaning Zakat is not obligatory on property lost for several years then found, so past years' Zakat is not due on him because it is legally non-existent. (2) His statement: 'And fallen into the sea'; meaning Zakat is not obligatory on property that fell into the sea and he extracted it after years. (3) His statement: 'And usurped'; meaning Zakat is not obligatory on property usurped by a man, and the owner has no evidence of the usurpation until it is possible for him to prove it and take it from him; so if he finds it after years, payment of past Zakat is not obligatory. However, if he has evidence, past Zakat becomes obligatory after he takes possession from the usurper, except in the case of usurpation of grazing livestock, then it is not obligatory, even if the usurper is admitting, because the condition of grazing is not established. As stated in al-Khaniyya. (4) His statement: 'In a desert' – with fatha on the bā', tashdīd on the yā': meaning a waterless desert. So if he buries property in the desert and forgets its place, then remembers it after years and extracts it, past Zakat is not obligatory, contrary to that buried in a house or garden and the like, then Zakat is obligatory on it because it is not dhimar. As stated in al-Bināya. (5) His statement: 'And a debt'; meaning if a man has a debt on another of the amount of nisab, and the debtor denies it, and the owner has no evidence against him, then the debtor admits after a period before people that it is due, then Zakat for the period of denial is not obligatory on him. (6) His statement: 'Confiscation'; meaning from the aspect of confiscation, which is what the ruler takes from his subjects unjustly. The difference between it and usurpation is that usurpation is taking property directly by force, while confiscation is that he orders him to bring such and such wealth. So what the ruler and similar ones take by confiscation, then return to him after years, past Zakat is not obligatory on him. With us, Zakat is not obligatory on mal al-dhimar, contrary to al-Shafi'i; based on the fact that it is not a condition for the obligation of Zakat that the nisab be owned both in ownership (raqaba) and possession (yad), and mal al-dhimar in its categories is not owned in possession though it is owned in ownership. And it is supported by what Abu Ubayd narrated in his Kitab al-Amwal with his chain from al-Hasan al-Basri who said: 'When the time comes when a man pays his Zakat, he pays for every property and every debt except that which is dhimar which he does not hope for.' And in the Muwatta of Malik from Ayyub: 'Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz wrote concerning property seized unjustly by some governors, ordering it to be returned to its owners, and its Zakat for past years to be taken. Then after that he followed it with a letter that only Zakat for one year should be taken from it, because it was dhimar.' And in the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba from Umar ibn Maymun who said: 'Al-Walid ibn Abd al-Malik took twenty thousand from a man from the people of Raqqa called Abu Aisha and placed it in the treasury. When Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz became caliph, his children came and raised their grievance to him. So he wrote to Maymun: Give their property to them and take the Zakat of this year from it. For had it not been dhimar, we would have taken from it Zakat for the past.' As stated in Takhrīj Ahādīth al-Hidāya by al-Zayla'i.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya's commentary on Sharh al-Wiqāya addresses the category of wealth known as 'al-māl al-ḍimār' (inaccessible wealth) and its Zakat ruling. The author first lists several examples: lost property, property sunken in the sea, usurped property without evidence, buried property in the desert whose location is forgotten, a debt denied for years then admitted, and wealth taken by confiscation and later returned. All these are instances of mal al-dhimar. The commentary then clarifies each example. The key legal point is that Zakat is not due on such wealth for the past years because effective possession (yad) is lacking. However, if the owner has evidence (e.g., for usurped property), Zakat becomes due upon recovery. An exception is made for grazing livestock (sā'ima) where even with evidence, past Zakat is not owed because the condition of being sā'ima is not established. The buried property ruling distinguishes between a desert (where Zakat is not due) and a house/garden (where Zakat is due) because the latter is not truly inaccessible. The passage then states the Hanafi position explicitly: 'With us, Zakat is not obligatory on mal al-dhimar, contrary to al-Shafi'i.' The basis of the ikhtilaf is whether effective possession is a condition for Zakat. Hanafis require both legal ownership (raqaba) and effective possession (yad); mal al-dhimar lacks yad. The Shafi'i school does not require yad, so Zakat would be due. The author provides supporting evidence from Hasan al-Basri's statement (transmitted by Abu Ubayd) and the practice of Caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, who instructed that only one year's Zakat be taken from confiscated wealth because it was dhimar. Another narration from the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba similarly shows Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz's judgment. Thus the Hanafi ruling is firmly grounded in precedent and legal reasoning.

Key Terms
المال الضمار (al-māl al-ḍimār): Wealth that is absent and not hoped to be obtained, i.e., inaccessible wealth.
يد (yad): Effective possession or control over the property, as opposed to mere legal ownership (raqaba).
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses, required to prove a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is Zakat due on usurped property that is later recovered?
A) Always due upon recovery, regardless of evidence.
B) Due only if the owner had evidence (bayyinah) of the usurpation.
C) Due only if the usurped property was grazing livestock (sā'ima).
D) Never due, because usurped property is always dhimar.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, Zakat is not obligated on mal al-dhimar (inaccessible wealth) because effective possession (yad) is a condition, and dhimar lacks yad.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Zakat on Lost Wealth (Mal al-Dhimar) – Definition and Ruling
Defines mal al-dhimar, states the Hanafi ruling with supporting athar, and contrasts with non-dhimar debts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-mal al-dhimar, explain the Hanafi ruling that zakat is not obligatory on it, and identify which debts are exceptions to this ruling.

Core Idea
Zakat requires complete ownership; lost wealth not hoped for lacks possession and thus no zakat.

This lesson examines the concept of al-mal al-dhimar—wealth that is absent and not expected to be recovered. The Hanafi school holds that such wealth does not attract zakat because the condition of complete ownership (milk tamm) is not met. We explore the definition, evidence from early Muslim authorities, and how this differs from other debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is zakat on lost wealth (al-mal al-dhimar). The Hanafi ruling: it is not obligatory (zakat is dropped).
The ruling is based on the absence of the condition of possession (yad).
2
Define 'al-mal al-dhimar'
It is wealth that is absent and not hoped to be obtained (la yurja husuluhu). It is owned in title (raqaba) but not in hand (yad).
The term derives from idhmar (concealment) or from a lean camel (ba'ir damir) that has life but no benefit.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
Three athar (narrations from early authorities) are cited: from al-Hasan al-Basri, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, and Umar ibn Maymun, all showing that zakat was not taken on dhimar wealth.
These are zanni in transmission but provide strong support for the Hanafi position.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that zakat is obligatory on dhimar wealth because ownership alone suffices; possession is not a condition.
The dispute is only when the wealth later reaches the owner—Shafi'i requires zakat for past years; Hanafi only for the current year.
5
Identify the exceptions (debts that are not dhimar)
Debts from a solvent (mali') or insolvent (mu'sir) or bankrupt (muflas) who admits, or from a denier with evidence, are not dhimar. When received, zakat for past years is obligatory.
The key is the ease (taysir) of recovery; if recovery is easy, it is not dhimar.

Source Text

عندنا لا تجب الزكاة في المال الضمار، خلافاً للشافعي؛ بناء على قوله : المالُ الضمار - بالكسر - : هو مالُ غائب لا يرجى حصوله، فإن فليس بضمار، وأصله من الإضمار : وهو التَّغييب والإخفاء، يقال: أضمر في قلبه شيء إذا لم يظهره وأخفاه، وقيل : هو ما يكون عينه قائماً، ولا يكون منتفعاً به، مأخوذ من قولهم : بعير ضامر : وهو الذي يكون فيه أصل الحياة ولا ينتفع به لشدة هزاله. كذا في «البناية». قوله : بناء؛ علة لقوله : «لا تجب»، يعني لا يشترط لوجوب الزكاة كون النصاب مملوكاً رقبة ويداً، والمالُ الضمار بأقسامه ليس بمملوك يداً وإن كان مملوكاً رقبة. ويؤيده ما أخرجه أبو عبيد في «كتاب الأموال» بسنده عن الحسن البصري له قال : «إذا حضر الوقت الذي يؤدِّي فيه الرَّجلُ زكاتَهُ أدَّى عن كلِّ مال، وعن كلِّ دين إلا ما كان منه ضماراً لا يرجوه». وفي «موطأ مالك» عن أيوب: «إنَّ عمر بن عبد العزيز له كتب في مال قبضَهُ بعض الولاة ظلماً يأمر برده إلى أهله، ويؤخذ زكاته لما مضى من السنين، ثم عقب بعد ذلك بكتاب أن لا يؤخذ منه إلا زكاة سنة واحدة، فإنه كان ضماراً». وفي «مصنف ابن أبي شيبة»: عن عمر بن ميمون، قال : «أخذ الوليد بن عبد الملك عن رجل من أهل الرقة يقال له أبو عائشة عشرين ألفاً فألقاه في بيت المال، فلمّا ولي عمر بن عبد العزيز الله أتاه ولده فرفعوا مظلمتهم إليه، فكتب إلى ميمون أن ادفعوا إليهم أموالهم، وخذوا زكاة عامهم هذا، فإنه لولا أن كان مالاً ضماراً أخذنا منه زكاة ما مضى). اشتراط الملك التّام، فهو مملواً رقبة لا يداً، والخلافُ فيما إذا وَصَلَ المالُ الضمار إلى مالكه، هل تجب عليه زكاة السنين التي كان المال فيها ضماراً أم لا ؟ (بخلاف دین على مقر مليء، أو معسر، أو مُفْلِس، أو جاحد عليه ببينة، أو عَلِمَ به قاض) فإنَّه إذا وَصَلَت هذه الأموال إلى مالكها تجب زكاةُ الأَيَّامِ الماضية. (ولا يبقى للتجارة) قوله : اشتراط الملك؛ لقوله : خُذ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ صَدَقَةً، المال المطلق منصرف إلى الفرد الكامل، وهو المملوك رقبة ويداً. [٢] قوله : فيما إذا وصل ... الخ؛ وأما إذا لم يصل إليه فلا خلاف في سقوطِ زكاته، وكذا لا خلاف في وجوب زكاته من يوم وصل إليه. [۳]قوله : بخلاف؛ شروع في حكم الأموال التي لا تعد من الضمار، وإن لم يكن في يد المالك حالاً. [٤] قوله : مليء بالهمزة، فعيل من الملأ، بمعنى الغنى، والحاصل أن تجبَ الزَّكاةُ في دين لـ له على غني يقر بكونه عليه، وكذا إذا كان على معسر : اسم فاعـل مـن الاعسار : أي فقير محتاج، وهو مقر، وكذا إذا كان مقرّر مفلس، وهو اسم مفعول من التفليس،، وهو الذي حكم الحاكم بإفلاسه، ونادى بأنه مفلس لا يلازم ولا يعاقب. وكذا إذا كان الدين على منكر وجوبه عليه للمالك عليه بينة يتيسر إثباته بها، أو كان القاضي يعلم بوجوب دينه عليه، ففي هذه الصُّوَرِ تيسر الوصول إلى المال بأداء المديون نفسه، أو بالمرافعة إلى القاضي وقضائه بالبينة أو بعلمه فتجب زكاة السنين الماضية عند وصول ذلك المال إلى المالك. قوله : ولا يبقى... الخ؛ شروع في مسائل متعلقة بوجوب الزكاة في أموال التجارة.
With us, zakat is not obligatory on lost wealth (al-mal al-dhimar), contrary to al-Shafi'i, based on his statement: al-mal al-dhimar (with kasrah) is wealth that is absent and not hoped to be obtained; if [it is hoped] then it is not dhimar. Its origin is from idhmar, which is concealing and hiding. It is said: something is idhmar in his heart if he does not display it and conceals it. And it is said: it is that whose essence exists but one cannot benefit from it, derived from their saying: a lean camel (ba'ir damir) which has the essence of life but is not benefited from due to severe emaciation. Thus in al-Binaya. His statement: 'based on' is the cause for his statement 'it is not obligatory', meaning that for the obligation of zakat it is not a condition that the nisab be owned in both title and possession, and lost wealth in its categories is not possessed in hand even though it is owned in title. And supporting that is what Abu Ubayd narrated in Kitab al-Amwal with his chain from al-Hasan al-Basri who said: 'When the time comes in which a man gives his zakat, he gives it for every wealth and every debt except what is dhimar that he does not hope for.' And in Muwatta Malik from Ayyub: 'Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz wrote concerning wealth that some governors had taken wrongfully ordering its return to its owners and taking its zakat for past years, then afterwards he followed that with a letter that only one year's zakat should be taken, for it was dhimar.' And in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba from Umar ibn Maymun, he said: 'Al-Walid ibn Abd al-Malik took twenty thousand from a man from al-Raqqa called Abu A'isha and put it in the treasury. Then when Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz became caliph, his children came and raised their grievance to him. So he wrote to Maymun: Give them their wealth and take the zakat of this current year, for were it not that it was dhimar wealth, we would have taken its zakat for the past.' [This is] as in Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidaya of al-Zayla'i. The condition of complete ownership, so it is owned in title not in hand. And the dispute is concerning when lost wealth reaches its owner, is he obligated to pay zakat for the years in which the wealth was dhimar or not? Contrary to a debt due from a solvent person who admits [the debt], or an insolvent person, or a bankrupt, or a denier against whom there is evidence, or a judge knows of it. For when these types of wealth reach their owner, zakat for the past days is obligatory. And it does not remain for trade. His statement: condition of ownership, due to His statement: 'Take from their wealth charity' (al-Tawba 103); the absolute word wealth refers to the complete individual, which is owned in title and hand. [2] His statement: 'concerning when it reaches...' etc. As for when it does not reach him, there is no disagreement that its zakat is dropped, and likewise no disagreement that its zakat is obligatory from the day it reaches him. [3] His statement: 'contrary to' is the start of the ruling on wealth that is not considered dhimar, even if not in the owner's hand currently. [4] His statement: 'solvent (mali')' with hamza, a fa'il form from mala' meaning wealth; the conclusion is that zakat is obligatory on a debt owed to him by a wealthy person who admits it, similarly if [the debtor] is insolvent (mu'sir) which is a fa'il from i'sar meaning poor and needy, and he admits; similarly if he is declared bankrupt (muflas) which is an ism maf'ul from taflees, and he is one whom the judge has declared bankrupt and announced that he is bankrupt and is not to be pursued or punished; similarly if the debt is against a denier, and the [creditor] has evidence that makes it easy to prove, or the judge knows of the debt. In these cases, access to the wealth is easy through the debtor's own payment, or by taking the matter to the judge and his judgment based on evidence or his knowledge, so zakat for past years is obligatory when that wealth reaches the owner. His statement: 'and it does not remain...' etc. is the start of issues related to the obligation of zakat on trade goods.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya is a commentary expanding on the ruling of zakat on 'lost wealth' (al-mal al-dhimar). The passage type is a ruling with proof, containing a definition, supporting narrations (athar), a brief inter-madhhab dispute, and an exception for debts. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: zakat is not obligatory on lost wealth (mal al-dhimar), contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The reason, based on the phrase 'based on his statement', is that zakat requires complete ownership (both title and possession). Lost wealth, by definition, is absent and not hoped to be obtained; it is owned in title (raqaba) but not in hand (yad). The commentator then provides three narrations to support this: from al-Hasan al-Basri (in Abu Ubayd's al-Amwal), from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz (in Muwatta Malik), and from Umar ibn Maymun (in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba), all showing that zakat is not taken on dhimar wealth. The passage then clarifies that the dispute with Shafi'i is only about whether zakat is due on past years when the wealth later reaches the owner; if it never reaches, all agree no zakat. Then it contrasts debts that are not considered dhimar: debts from a solvent, insolvent, bankrupt, or denier with evidence, where zakat is due when received. The commentator provides a detailed explanation of each term (mali', mu'sir, muflas). Finally, it transitions to trade goods. This shows the Hanafi school's precise conditions for zakat obligation, emphasizing actual possession for complete ownership.

Key Terms
المال الضمار (al-mal al-dhimar): Wealth that is absent and not hoped for (la yurja husuluhu); it may exist in essence but one cannot benefit from it, like a lean camel (ba'ir damir). It is owned in title (raqaba) but not in hand (yad).
ملاء (mala'): Wealth; from it is derived mali' (solvent), meaning wealthy.
مفلس (muflas): One who is declared bankrupt by a judge; his debt is treated as dhimar if recovery is difficult.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the key factor that makes wealth 'al-mal al-dhimar' such that zakat is not obligatory?
A) The wealth is less than the nisab.
B) The wealth is owned in title but not in hand and is not hoped to be recovered.
C) The wealth is used for trade.
D) The wealth is owned by a non-Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Lost wealth (mal al-dhimar) that is not hoped for does not incur zakat because the condition of possession (yad) is missing; only wealth fully owned in both title and hand triggers the obligation.

Zakat on Debts: When Past Years Are Due Upon Receipt
Explains when zakat is due on debts received from various debtors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between dhimar and non-dhimar debts and state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for past years when such debts are received.

Core Idea
Zakat for past years is due when the debt is collectible, not when it is dhimar

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi zakat law: whether a creditor must pay zakat for previous years when a debt is finally repaid. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that the obligation depends on the type of debtor and the ease of collection. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly calculating past zakat on receivables.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition for past zakat
The passage states that complete ownership (ملك تام) requires both title (رقبة) and possession (يد). For zakat to be due for past years, the creditor must have had the ability to collect the debt during those years.
This condition is derived from the Qur'anic verse 9:103.
2
Distinguish dhimar from non-dhimar debts
A dhimar debt is one that is difficult to collect due to the debtor's insolvency or evasion. The passage contrasts dhimar with debts where collection is possible through the debtor's admission or judicial enforcement.
The dispute (khilaf) revolves around whether zakat for past years is due on dhimar debts; the Hanafi position is that it is not.
3
List the five categories of non-dhimar debtors
The passage explicitly lists five types of debtors that are not dhimar: (1) solvent debtor who admits (mali'), (2) insolvent debtor who admits (mu'sir), (3) bankrupt declared by judge (muflis), (4) denier with evidence (jahid), (5) any debt known to the judge. For these, when the debt is received, zakat for past years is wajib.
In each case, the creditor has a reasonable expectation of collection.
4
Understand the evidence from Qur'an and its application
The proof cited is Quran 9:103 'Take from their wealth charity', where 'wealth' (مال) is interpreted as fully owned property – both title and possession. A dhimar debt lacks the possession element in the past years, so zakat is not due for those years. For non-dhimar debts, the possession element is considered existent because collection was possible.
This is a dalil naqli that supports the Hanafi position.

اشتراط الملك التّام، فهو مملواً رقبة لا يداً، والخلافُ فيما إذا وَصَلَ المالُ الضمار إلى مالكه، هل تجب عليه زكاة السنين التي كان المال فيها ضماراً أم لا؟ بخلاف دين على مقر مليء، أو معسر، أو مُفلس، أو جاحد عليه ببينة، أو علم به قاض فإنَّه إذا وَصَلَت هذه الأموال إلى مالكها تجب زكاةُ الأَيَّامِ الماضية. قوله: اشتراط الملك؛ لقوله: خُذ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ صَدَقَةً، المال المطلق منصرف إلى الفرد الكامل، وهو المملوك رقبة ويداً. وأما إذا لم يصل إليه فلا خلاف في سقوطِ زكاته، وكذا لا خلاف في وجوب زكاته من يوم وصل إليه. قوله: بخلاف؛ شروع في حكم الأموال التي لا تعد من الضمار، وإن لم يكن في يد المالك حالاً. قوله: مليء بالهمزة، فعيل من الملأ، بمعنى الغنى، والحاصل أن تجبَ الزَّكاةُ في دين له على غني يقر بكونه عليه، وكذا إذا كان على معسر: اسم فاعل من الإعسار: أي فقير محتاج، وهو مقر، وكذا إذا كان مقرّر مفلس، وهو اسم مفعول من التفليس، وهو الذي حكم الحاكم بإفلاسه، ونادى بأنه مفلس لا يلازم ولا يعاقب. وكذا إذا كان الدين على منكر وجوبه عليه للمالك عليه بينة يتيسر إثباته بها، أو كان القاضي يعلم بوجوب دينه عليه، ففي هذه الصُّورِ تيسر الوصول إلى المال بأداء المديون نفسه، أو بالمرافعة إلى القاضي وقضائه بالبينة أو بعلمه فتجب زكاة السنين الماضية عند وصول ذلك المال إلى المالك.
The condition of complete ownership: it is owned in title but not in possession. And the dispute concerns when a dhimar (hard-to-collect) debt reaches its owner: is zakat for the years in which the debt was dhimar obligatory or not? Unlike a debt owed by a solvent debtor who admits, or an insolvent one, or a bankrupt, or a denier against whom there is evidence, or a judge knows of it. For when these funds reach their owner, zakat for the past days is obligatory. He said: the condition of ownership; due to His saying: 'Take from their wealth charity'; the absolute wealth is directed to the perfect individual, i.e., owned in title and possession. As for if it does not reach him, there is no disagreement that its zakat is dropped, and likewise no disagreement about its zakat being obligatory from the day it reaches him. He said: 'unlike'; start of the ruling on funds that are not considered dhimar, even if not in the owner's hand presently. He said: 'mali' (solvent) with a hamza, from mala' meaning wealth; the conclusion is that zakat is obligatory in a debt owed to him by a wealthy person who admits it, and likewise if it is upon a mu'sir (insolvent): a needy person who admits, and likewise if it is upon a muflis (bankrupt) whom the judge declared bankrupt and announced as bankrupt, not to be prosecuted or punished. And likewise if the debt is upon a denier of its obligation against whom the owner has clear evidence that can be confirmed, or the judge knows of the debt's obligation. In these cases, access to the money is easy, either by the debtor paying himself or by litigation before the judge who rules by evidence or his knowledge. So the zakat of the past years is obligatory when that money reaches the owner.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage type is a ruling with proof concerning the obligation of zakat on debts that are received after a delay. The text first states the condition of complete ownership (ملك تام) — ownership both in title (رقبة) and possession (يد). It then presents a dispute (خلاف) regarding dhimar (ضمار), i.e., a debt that is difficult to collect: when such a debt eventually reaches the owner, is zakat due for the past years? The Hanafi position, as clarified subsequently, is that zakat is not due for those past years because the owner did not have full possession. The text then contrasts (بخلاف) with certain types of debts that are not considered dhimar. For these, when the money is received, zakat for the past years is obligatory. The debtors that trigger this obligation are: (1) a solvent debtor (مليء) who admits the debt; (2) an insolvent debtor (معسر) who admits; (3) a bankrupt (مفلس) declared by a judge; (4) a denier (جاحد) against whom the owner has evidence (بينة); (5) the judge knows of the debt. The rationale is that in these cases, the owner can reasonably expect to collect the debt, either through the debtor's voluntary payment or through judicial enforcement. The proof for the condition of ownership is drawn from the Qur'anic verse 'Take from their wealth charity' (9:103), where 'wealth' (مال) is understood as fully owned property — both title and physical possession. The commentary explains each debtor type: 'mali'' is from 'mala'' meaning wealth; 'mu'sir' is from 'i'sar' meaning poverty; 'muflis' is the one officially bankrupted; 'jāhid' is the denier. The key ruling: when the debt reaches the owner from any of these categories, zakat for all past years since the debt originated is wajib (obligatory). This is a mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
ضمار (dhimār): A debt that is difficult to collect because of the debtor's insolvency or evasion.
مليء (malī’): A solvent debtor who admits the debt.
معسر (mu‘sir): An insolvent debtor who admits the debt but lacks funds.
جاحد (jāḥid): A debtor who denies the debt but against whom the creditor has evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only when the debtor is solvent and admits the debt.
B) When the debtor is solvent, insolvent but admits, bankrupt, denier with evidence, or the judge knows.
C) Only when the debtor is bankrupt and the judge declares it.
D) For all debts, regardless of the debtor's status.

Key Takeaway
Zakat for past years is due upon receipt only if the debt was not dhimar, i.e., the debtor was solvent, admitted, bankrupt, deniable with evidence, or known to the judge.

Trade Goods and Change of Intention
States when purchased items become trade goods and effect of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when purchased goods become trade goods for zakat and explain the effect of changing intention, including the limitation on reverting to trade status.

Core Idea
Intention alone determines trade goods at purchase; once removed, only sale or rent restores trade status.

This lesson explores a critical nuance in Hanafi zakat law: how intention interacts with the classification of trade goods. The source passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya establishes that while initial intention can designate an item for trade, a subsequent change to personal use permanently removes it from the zakatable category unless a new commercial act occurs. It also clarifies that assets acquired through non-commercial means (inheritance, gift, etc.) never become trade goods merely by intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Initial Ruling
Locate the ruling that an item purchased with the intention of trade becomes a trade good subject to zakat. Note the hadith cited (innama al-a'mal bi-l-niyyat) as evidence.
The initial purchase with trade intention triggers zakat obligation if nisab and haul conditions are met.
2
Analyze the Effect of Changing Intention
Examine the ruling that intending service (khidma) removes the item from trade, citing the same hadith. Understand why intention can both create and remove trade status.
The hadith applies to the initial classification and to a change that is diametrically opposed (trade -> service).
3
Understand the Reversion Limitation
Study the statement that once removed, the item cannot revert to trade by a second intention alone; only sale or rent can restore trade status. Identify the rationale: preventing circumvention of zakat.
This is a restriction on the scope of the hadith: intention cannot reclassify without a new commercial transaction.
4
Examine Non-Commercial Acquisitions
List the modes of acquisition that do not become trade goods even if trade is intended: inheritance, gift, bequest, marriage dower, khul', and settlement for qawad. Understand why intention is insufficient here.
These are non-voluntary or non-commercial origins; the Hanafi school strictly limits trade goods to purchase with intent.
5
Apply the Rules to a Scenario
Given a concrete scenario (e.g., buying a slave for trade, then intending service, then wanting to sell), trace the rulings step by step to confirm the fiqh outcome.
Practice builds fluency with the nuanced interaction of intention and commercial acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يبقى للتجارة: شروع في مسائل متعلقة بوجوب الزكاة في أموال التجارة. قوله: فنوى خدمته؛ يعني إذا اشترى عبداً أو أمةً بنية التجارة ثم نوى إخراجه من التجارة وجعله لخدمته خرج ذلك عن التجارة ولم تجب فيه الزكاة، فإن لكل امرئ ما نوى، وإنما الأعمال بالنيات، أخرجه البخاري وغيره. قوله: ثم لا يصير؛ أي إذا أخرج عبداً ونحوه عن التجارة ونوى خدمته لا يصير أبداً للتجارة، وإن نواه ثانياً لها، إلا أن يبيعه أو يؤجره. قوله: وما اشتراه؛ أي ما اشتري بنية التجارة كان للتجارة تجب فيه الزكاة. قوله: لا ما ورثه؛ يعني المال الذي يحصل في ملكه بالسبب غير الاختياري كالوراثة، بأن مات مورثه فورث ماله لا يصير للتجارة وإن نواها عند التملك. قوله: وما ملكه بهبة؛ بأن وهب له إنسان مالاً وقبضه. قوله: وصية؛ بأن أوصى رجل عند موته لرجل فوصل إليه بعد موت الموصي. قوله: أو بنكاح؛ بأن نكح امرأة وأعطى في مهرها مالاً. قوله: أو خُلع؛ بأن خالعت المرأة عن زوجها، وطلبت منه الطلاق وأعطته مالاً. قوله: أو صلح؛ بأن قتل رجل رجلاً عمداً فوجب عليه القصاص فصالح القاتل مع ورثة المقتول على مال أعطاه إياهم. قوله: قود؛ بفتحتين: أي القصاص قيد به؛ لأن العبد للتجارة إذا قتله عبد...
And it does not remain for trade: a start in matters related to the obligation of zakat on trade assets. He said: 'so he intended service'; that is, if one buys a male or female slave with the intention of trade, then intends to remove it from trade and make it for service, it leaves trade and zakat is not obligatory on it, for every person has what he intended, and actions are by intentions, narrated by Bukhari and others. He said: 'then it does not become'; that is, if he removes a slave and the like from trade and intends service, it never becomes trade again, even if he intends it for trade a second time, unless he sells it or rents it. He said: 'and what he bought'; that is, what is bought with the intention of trade is for trade, zakat is obligatory on it. He said: 'not what he inherited'; that is, wealth that comes into his ownership by non-voluntary means like inheritance: if his testator dies and he inherits his wealth, it does not become for trade even if he intends it for trade at the time of ownership. He said: 'and what he owns by gift'; that is, if someone gives him wealth and he takes possession of it. He said: 'by bequest'; that is, if a man at his death bequeaths to a man, and it reaches him after the testator's death. He said: 'or by marriage'; that is, if he marries a woman and gives wealth as her dower. He said: 'or by khul''; that is, if the woman divorces herself from her husband by khul', asking him for divorce and giving him wealth. He said: 'or by sulh'; that is, if a man kills another willfully, so retaliation is obligatory, and the killer makes a settlement with the heirs of the deceased for wealth he gives them. He said: 'qawad' with two fathas: that is, retaliation; it is restricted because a slave used for trade, if he kills a slave...
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage type is a ruling with proof concerning the status of goods intended for trade and the effect of changing intention. The text begins by introducing the topic: 'and it does not remain for trade' signals the discussion of zakat on trade assets. The key ruling is: if a person purchases a commodity (e.g., a slave) with the intention of trade, then later changes his intention to use it for personal service (خدمة), the commodity exits the category of trade goods and zakat is no longer obligatory on it. This is based on the prophetic hadith 'إنما الأعمال بالنيات' (actions are by intentions), which establishes the legal effect of intention. However, a critical nuance: once an item is removed from trade by intending service, it cannot revert to trade status merely by a second intention. It only becomes trade goods again if the owner actually sells it or rents it out. This prevents easy circumvention of zakat by toggling intention. The text then lists various ways of acquiring property that do not automatically make it trade goods even if the owner intends trade: inheritance (وراثة), gift (هبة), bequest (وصية), dower from marriage (نكاح), khul' (خلع), and settlement for retaliation (صلح عن قود). In all these cases, the acquisition is by a non-voluntary or non-commercial cause, so mere intention does not make them trade assets; they require a commercial transaction (purchase with intention of trade). The hadith is cited as evidence that intention matters, but for these acquisitions, the intention is not sufficient because the mode of acquisition itself is not trade. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that intention can transform an act of worship but not the legal category of an asset unless accompanied by a commercial exchange.

Key Terms
التجارة (al-tijāra): Trade; the purchase and sale of goods with the intention of profit, making them subject to zakat.
الخدمة (al-khidma): Personal service; using a purchased slave or item for one's own benefit rather than for resale.
قود (qawad): Retaliation (qisas) in cases of intentional killing where the killer may settle with heirs for a payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how can an item that was removed from trade (e.g., a slave intended for service) become a trade good again?
A) By the owner changing his intention back to trade.
B) By selling it or renting it out.
C) By using it in a commercial transaction involving exchange.
D) It can never become a trade good again.

Key Takeaway
An asset becomes a trade good only if purchased with trade intention; once removed, intention alone cannot restore it; acquisitions via non-commercial means never become trade goods by intention alone.

Intention and Acquisition Modes for Trade Goods
Sharh explaining when intention makes property trade goods for zakah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on when intention converts property into trade goods subject to zakah, and identify which acquisition modes (purchase vs non-purchase) affect that status.

Core Idea
Intention alone cannot turn inherited wealth into trade goods; only purchase with trade intention creates zakah liability.

This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘ayah explains a key ruling of zakah on trade goods (urud al-tijarah). It clarifies that while intention is central, not all acquisitions respond to intention equally. Understanding the distinction between voluntary and non-voluntary modes of ownership is essential for correct determination of zakah obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the effect of intending a purchased slave for service: the slave ceases to be a trade good, and even if later intended for trade again, it does not become a trade good until sold or rented.
This shows the strength of the removal act.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith 'Actions are by intentions' is cited as the proof that intention governs the trade status of property.
This dalil is used broadly; specific applications are derived through qiyas.
3
Contrast purchase with non-purchase acquisitions
Properties acquired through inheritance, gift, will, marriage dower, khul', or sulh for qisas do not become trade goods by intention alone, unlike purchase.
The mode of acquisition matters: voluntary purchase vs non-voluntary receipt.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding compensation received for a killed trade slave: does it become a trade good if intended?
Abu Yusuf says yes, Muhammad says no; the relied-upon view is Abu Yusuf's.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later Hanafi masters prefer Abu Yusuf's position, so the compensation becomes a trade good if intended.
This highlights the importance of niyyah even for non-exchange acquisitions.

ونواه لها كان لها عند أبي يوسف الله ، لا عند محمد ه ، وقيل : الخلاف على عكسه
ونواه لها كان لها عند أبي يوسف له ، لا عند محمد) ، وقيل : الخلاف على عكسه، فالحاصل أن ما عدا الحجرين والسوائم إنما تَجِبُ فيها الزَّكاةُ بنية التجارة". خطأ أو دفع به كان المدفوع للتجارة. كذا في «الخانية».
What he purchased for it (trade) then intended for its service, then it does not become for trade even if he intends it for it as long as he does not sell it. And what he purchased for it was for it, not what he inherited and intended for it. And what he owned by gift, or will, or marriage, or khul', or sulh in lieu of qawad. [2] 'What he purchased for it then intended for its service', then it does not become for trade even if he intends it for it as long as he does not sell it. And what he purchased for it was for it, not what he inherited and intended for it. And what he owned by gift, or will, or marriage, or khul', or sulh. [1] His saying: 'then intended for its service' means if he purchased a slave or slave girl with the intention of trade, then intended to remove it from trade and make it for service, it leaves trade and zakat is not obligatory on it, because ‘every person has what he intended, and actions are by intentions’—recorded by Bukhari and others. (2) His saying: 'then it does not become' means if he removes a slave and similar from trade and intends it for service, it never becomes for trade, even if he intends it for it a second time, unless he sells it or rents it. [3] His saying: 'and what he purchased' means what he purchased with the intention of trade is for trade—zakat is obligatory on it. (4) His saying: 'not what he inherited' means wealth that comes into his ownership by non-voluntary means like inheritance—if his testator dies and he inherits his wealth, it does not become for trade even if he intends it. [0] His saying: 'and what he owned by gift' means if a person gave him wealth and he took possession. [6] His saying: 'will' means if a man bequeathed to another at his death, and it reached him after the testator's death. [7] His saying: 'or by marriage' means he married a woman and gave wealth as her dower. [8] His saying: 'or khul'' means the woman divorced her husband, asked him for divorce, and gave him wealth. [9] His saying: 'or sulh' means a man intentionally killed another, so qisas became obligatory on him, and the killer reconciled with the heirs of the slain for wealth he gave them. [10] His saying: 'qawad' with two fathas: i.e., qisas, restricted by it because a trade slave if killed by a slave... (1) Meaning if he removes a slave and similar from trade and intends it for service, it never becomes for trade, even if he intends it for it, unless he sells it or rents it. See al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:10). (2) Al-Qawad: al-qisas. See al-Qamus (1:343). (3) In Sahih al-Bukhari (2:893), and the second sentence of the hadith is mentioned before the first. And if he intends it for trade, it is for trade according to Abu Yusuf, not according to Muhammad; and it is said the disagreement is the opposite. And if he intends it for trade, it is for trade according to Abu Yusuf, not according to Muhammad; and it is said the disagreement is the opposite. The conclusion: apart from gold, silver, and grazing livestock, zakat is only obligatory on them with the intention of trade. An error or payment made with it, what is paid becomes for trade. Thus in al-Khaniyya.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the matn (Wiqayah) on the rules of urud al-tijarah (trade goods) concerning intention (niyyah) and modes of acquisition. It first states the ruling: if someone purchases a slave with the intention of trade, then later intends to take him out of trade for personal service, the slave ceases to be a trade good. Even if the owner later intends him once more for trade, he does not become a trade good again unless he sells or rents him out. This is because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said, 'Actions are by intentions,' and also because the removal from trade is a strong act of deactivation. The text then clarifies that only property acquired by purchase with trade intention becomes a trade good subject to zakah. Property obtained through inheritance, gift, will, marriage dower, khul' compensation, or sulh (settlement) from qisas does not become a trade good by mere intention, even if the owner intends it for trade upon receipt. The commentary provides examples for each mode. The passage then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a trade slave who is killed and the owner receives blood money or compensation with the intention of trade. According to Abu Yusuf, such money becomes a trade good and is subject to zakah, while Imam Muhammad holds the opposite view. Some reports reverse the positions. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf, as noted in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. The conclusion is that for all goods other than gold, silver, and sā'imah (grazing livestock), zakah is obligatory only when accompanied by the intention of trade. If someone mistakenly or intentionally pays something (e.g., as compensation) and receives it with trade intention, it becomes a trade good.

Key Terms
قود (qawad): Here means 'retaliation' (qisas) in cases of intentional killing; the compensation paid to avoid qisas.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
صلح (sulh): Settlement agreement, here specifically between a killer and the heirs of a victim to avoid qisas by paying compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following acquisitions can become a trade good by intention alone?
A) Inheritance
B) Gift
C) Purchase
D) Khul'

Key Takeaway
Property acquired through non-purchase means (inheritance, gift, will, marriage dower, khul', sulh) never becomes a trade good by intention alone; purchase with trade intention is required.
