Evidence for Zakah on Trade Goods: The Necessity of Intention
Proofs for intention requirement in trade goods zakah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the primary evidences (Quran, ijma, hadith) for the Hanafi ruling that zakah on trade goods requires intention and explain how each proof supports the ruling.

Core Idea
Intention (niyyah) is the decisive factor: trade goods are zakahable only when acquired with the intent to trade.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents the textual and consensus-based proofs for a key Hanafi ruling: that zakah on trade goods ('urud al-tijarah) becomes obligatory only when the owner held the intention (niyyah) of trade at the time of acquisition. Without that intention, even if the goods are later used for trade, they may not be subject to zakah. The commentator marshals evidence from the Quran, the consensus of the majority, prophetic practice, and the conduct of the early caliphs. Understanding these proofs helps you appreciate why the Hanafi school places such emphasis on the trader's original intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
First, identify the exact ruling being proven: that zakah on trade goods is wajib only when accompanied by the intention of trade (niyyat al-tijarah).
The ruling is about a condition for obligation, not about the zakah itself.
2
Locate the Quranic Proof
Find the Quranic verse cited (2:267) and identify the phrase used as evidence: 'Spend from the good things you have earned.' Understand how it is linked to trade profits and zakah.
The verse is general; its application to trade goods requires supporting evidence.
3
Analyze the Consensus Claim
The commentator asserts consensus (ijma') of the majority of scholars. Note how he treats any dissenting view as 'aberrant and rejected' (shad wa mardud).
In Hanafi usul, ijma is a binding proof.
4
Examine the Supporting Hadith
Evaluate the three hadith references: (1) Samurah's hadith (weak chain), (2) the practice of Umar as narrated by Ziyad ibn Hudayr, and (3) the story of Khalid ibn al-Walid about waqf. For each, note the strength and the specific point it supports.
Weak hadith can still be used as corroborating evidence (mutabi'), especially when combined with other proofs.
5
Synthesize the Argument
Combine all proofs: the Quran provides the general obligation to spend from earnings, ijma confirms inclusion of trade goods, and the secondary reports (especially the Khalid story) show that intention is the distinguishing factor. Conclude that intention is a necessary condition.
This is a typical Hanafi approach: use multiple strands of evidence to establish a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بنية التجارة ؛ الأصلُ في وجوب الزَّكاةِ بنية التجارة قوله عملة : أَنفِقُوا من طَيِّبَاتِ مَا كَسَبْتُمْ (٢) ، وعليه إجماع جمهور علماء الأمة سلفاً وخلفاً، وقولُ المخالف فيه شاد ومردود كما بسطَه النووي في «شرح صحيح مسلم» وغيره. ويؤيده حديث سمرة : كان رسولُ الله يأمرنا أن نخرج الزكاة مما نعد للبيع»، أخرجه أبو داودَ والدَّارَ قُطْنِيُّ والبزار بسندٍ فيه ضعف. وعـن زيـاد بـن حدير له : بعثني عمر الله مصدقاً، فأمرني أن آخذ من المسلمين من أموالهم إذا اختلفوا بها للتجارة ربع العشر ، ومن أموال أهل الذمة نصف العشر، ومن أموال أهل الحرب العشر»، أخرجه أبو عبيد في «كتاب الأموال». (٤) وروى عبد الرزاق والطبراني وغيرهما بمعناه ) ، ويشهد له حديث الصحيحين في قصة منع خالد زكاة عروضه وشهادة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بأنه ليست في أمواله زكاة ؛ لأنه جعلها وقفاً في سبيل الله ، وللتفصيل موضع آخر.
His saying: 'with the intention of trade'; the basis for the obligation of zakah with the intention of trade is His statement: 'Spend from the good things you have earned' (Quran 2:267). Upon this is the consensus of the majority of scholars of the ummah, past and later. The view of the dissenter is aberrant and rejected, as al-Nawawi detailed in his commentary on Sahih Muslim and elsewhere. It is supported by the hadith of Samurah: 'The Messenger of Allah used to command us to give zakah on what we prepared for sale'—recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Daraqutni, and al-Bazzar with a chain containing weakness. And from Ziyad ibn Hudayr: 'Umar sent me as a collector and commanded me to take from the Muslims a quarter of a tenth from their wealth if they traded with it, from the dhimmis half a tenth, and from the harbis a tenth'—recorded by Abu Ubayd in Kitab al-Amwal. (4) And Abd al-Razzaq, al-Tabarani, and others narrated similarly. It is supported by the hadith in the Sahihayn about the story of Khalid withholding zakah on his goods and the Prophet's testimony that there is no zakah on his wealth because he made it a waqf in the path of Allah. For details, another place.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage constitutes the proof section for the rule that zakah on trade goods ('urud al-tijarah) is obligatory only when accompanied by intention (niyyah). The commentator begins by stating the basis: the Quranic verse (2:267) commands spending from good earnings, which includes trade profits. He then asserts that the majority of scholars across generations agree (ijma') that trade goods are subject to zakah with intention. Any opposing view is considered aberrant and rejected, as al-Nawawi explained. Then, supporting hadiths are cited: the hadith of Samurah (though its chain is weak) that the Prophet ordered zakah on goods prepared for sale; the practice of Caliph Umar as narrated by Ziyad ibn Hudayr, who took zakah from Muslim traders at the rate of 1/4 of a tenth (2.5%), from dhimmis half a tenth, and from harbis a tenth; and additional narrations from Abd al-Razzaq and al-Tabarani. Finally, the famous hadith from the Sahihayn about Khalid ibn al-Walid is mentioned: when some accused Khalid of not paying zakah on his camels, the Prophet testified that there was no zakah on them because Khalid had made them a waqf (endowment) in Allah's path. This shows that if goods are not intended for trade (here they are waqf), zakah is not due, implying that for trade goods, intention is essential. This indirect evidence supports the principle.

Key Terms
بنية التجارة (bi-niyyat al-tijarah): The condition or requirement of having the intention of trade when acquiring or holding goods for them to be subject to zakah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT cited as evidence for the obligation of zakah on trade goods with intention in this passage?
A) The Quranic verse 'Spend from the good things you have earned'
B) The hadith of Samurah commanding zakah on goods prepared for sale
C) The practice of Caliph Umar in collecting zakah from traders
D) The hadith that 'There is no zakah on camels'

Key Takeaway
Intention (niyyat al-tijarah) is a necessary condition for zakah on trade goods, proven by Quran, consensus, and the practice of the Prophet and the Companions.

Conditions for Zakah on Trade Goods: Niyyah and Cause of Acquisition
Explains the ruling on intention for trade in zakah and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for zakah on trade goods and apply them to distinguish between optional and non-optional acquisitions.

Core Idea
Zakah on trade goods requires intention of trade at the moment of optional acquisition — inheritance does not trigger trade zakah.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on when trade goods become liable to zakah. You will learn that intention (niyyah) is not enough by itself; it must coincide with a voluntary acquisition. We will examine the proof from Quran and Sunnah, and see why inherited property, even if intended for sale, is not subject to trade zakah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that for items other than gold, silver, and grazing livestock, zakah is only obligatory with intention of trade. This is the foundational principle.
Gold and silver (as cash) and grazing livestock are excluded because their zakah is based on other mechanisms.
2
Examine the proofs
The author cites Quran 2:267, consensus of the majority, and hadiths from Samurah and the practice of Umar to establish the obligation.
The hadith of Samurah has a weak chain but is supported by other evidence, so it is used as corroboration.
3
Understand the timing condition
Intention must exist at the time the cause of ownership occurs. If you acquire an item for personal use and later decide to sell it, the later intention does not make it trade goods for zakah.
The phrase 'then it does not become for trade, even if he intended it' encapsulates this rule.
4
Classify the cause as optional or non-optional
The cause of ownership must be optional (ikhtiyari) — a voluntary action like purchase or gift. Non-optional causes like inheritance (idtirari) do not allow the intention of trade to be effective, even if present at the moment of acquisition.
Inheritance occurs without any action by the heir; thus there is no 'work' (san') to which the intention can attach.
5
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that any optional cause suffices, while Muhammad al-Shaybani requires it to be a purchase. The relied-upon position is that any optional acquisition works, but there is a difference of opinion.
This ikhtilaf is recorded in the text and resolved in later works like al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(3) In Sunan Abi Dawud (1) (488), Ma'rifat al-Sunan (7): (13), and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (4:146), etc. (4) And from Abu Dharr: 'On wheat, sadaqah' reported by Ahmad, al-Daraqutni, and al-Hakim with a good chain. See al-Dirayah (1:261), and from Ibn Umar: 'There is no zakah on goods except what is for trade' in Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (4:147), and he authenticated it. Then this intention is only considered if it exists at the time of the occurrence of the cause of ownership, so that if he intends trade after the occurrence of the cause of ownership, zakah is not obligatory on it with intention of trade, and this is the meaning of his statement: then it does not become for trade, even if he intended it. Then the cause of ownership must be an optional cause, so that if he intends trade at the time of his ownership by inheritance, zakah is not obligatory on it. Then that optional cause, must it be purchase or not? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no. (1) His statement: only considered; because if the intention is connected to the action, it is necessary to consider it; since intention is to distinguish different types of action, it must be at the occurrence of the cause of ownership. [2] His statement: after the occurrence of the cause of ownership; such as one who purchases a slave for service then intends trade in it, or purchased it for trade then annulled it by making it for service, then intended it for trade. [3] His statement: not obligatory; it is said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'The condition is its concurrence with the contract of trade, which is earning wealth with wealth by a contract of purchase, lease, or borrowing, and even if he intends trade after the contract or buys something for personal use intending that if he finds profit, no zakah is on him, as if he intends trade on what comes out of his land.' [4] His statement: an optional cause; the cause of ownership is of two types: one: by the choice and act of the servant: which depends on offer and acceptance, and is invalidated by cancellation, like purchase, acceptance of gift, bequest, charity, khul', settlement, and other causes of ownership. The second: that which involves no choice of the servant: like inheritance, for inheritance enters the owner's possession without action, so that even the fetus inherits without action, and it is not dropped by renunciation. If you know this, then know that the intention of trade is only considered when it accompanies action, and there is no action in the non-optional cause, so the concurrence of intention with it does not benefit anything. [0] His statement: no; meaning it is not necessary that it be purchase, but rather every action that causes ownership if... (1) End from al-Durr al-Mukhtar (2:274).
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on al-Wiqayah, discusses the conditions for zakah on trade goods (urud al-tijarah). The passage is a ruling with proof, opening with the general principle that zakah on items other than gold, silver, and grazing livestock is only obligatory if the owner intended them for trade. The author then provides evidence: the Quranic verse 'Spend from the good things you have earned' (2:267), consensus of the majority, and several hadiths, including a command from the Prophet to give zakah on goods prepared for sale and the practice of Umar in collecting zakah on trade goods. The commentary notes a disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding a specific scenario (the text is unclear), but the relied-upon position is that the intention for trade is essential.

The passage then explains that the intention of trade must exist at the time the cause of ownership occurs. For example, if one buys an item for personal use and later decides to sell it, the new intention does not make it trade goods for zakah purposes. The cause of ownership must be optional (ikhtiyari), such as purchase, gift, bequest, etc., and not forced like inheritance. For inheritance, even if one intends to trade the inherited item at the moment of receiving it, no zakah is due. The author then raises the question of whether the optional cause must specifically be purchase or any transaction that transfers ownership. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that it need not be purchase; any optional acquisition suffices, while the text cuts off before stating Muhammad's view, but from other sources Muhammad requires purchase. The passage concludes by emphasizing that intention must accompany the voluntary action (san') of acquisition, as there is no action in involuntary causes like inheritance. The reliance on al-Durr al-Mukhtar shows that the operative ruling is that intention must coincide with the contract of trade.

Key Terms
نِيَّة التِّجَارَة (niyyat al-tijarah): Intention to engage in trade as a business at the time of acquiring an asset, making it subject to zakah as trade goods.
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): The cause or means by which ownership is acquired, such as purchase, gift, or inheritance.
اختياري (ikhtiyari): Acquisition by choice and action of the person, such as buying or accepting a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of intending trade for property acquired through inheritance?
A) Zakah becomes obligatory because the intention is valid.
B) Zakah is not obligatory because inheritance is not an optional cause.
C) Zakah is obligatory only if the inherited property is gold or silver.
D) Zakah depends on the value exceeding the nisab.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, zakah on trade goods is only due if the intention for trade coincides with an optional acquisition; inheritance and other non-optional causes do not generate trade zakah even with intention.

Intention for Trade Goods: Must Acquisition Be a Purchase?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on intention for trade goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the views of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the required type of acquisition for making trade goods zakatable, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
For zakat on trade goods, intention must accompany acquisition; later Hanafi preference requires purchase, not any voluntary contract.

This passage delves into a classic intra-Hanafi debate: when exactly does intention for trade turn an asset into zakatable trade goods? All Hanafi masters agree that intention must coincide with the acquisition of the property. But a disagreement arises over whether any voluntary acquisition suffices or only a purchase. The text presents the reasoning of both sides and ultimately points to the preferred view of Muhammad al-Shaybani, adopted by later Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared principle
Recognise that all Hanafi scholars agree intention for trade must coincide with the moment of acquiring ownership. Intention formed after acquisition does not make the goods trade goods.
This is the first condition for zakat on 'urud al-tijarah.
2
Recognise the first condition: voluntary cause
The cause of ownership must be voluntary (ikhtiyari), not involuntary like inheritance. If you inherit goods and intend to trade, no zakat is due.
Inheritance is an involuntary cause.
3
Locate the dispute
Within voluntary causes, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say any voluntary contract (gift, will, compromise) suffices if accompanied by trade intention. Muhammad restricts it to purchase only.
This is the core ikhtilaf in the text.
4
Follow each argument
Abu Hanifa argues that trade is defined as acquiring wealth by contract; any voluntary acquisition is a form of trade. Muhammad argues that contracts other than purchase (e.g., gift, compromise) are not considered trade dealings—proof: a mudarib or authorized slave, permitted to trade, cannot engage in these non-purchase contracts.
Muhammad's argument relies on the scope of permission to trade.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi authorities (al-Kasani, Ibn Nujaym) considered Muhammad's view the most correct (al-ashah). The operative ruling for fatwa is that only purchase makes goods zakatable with trade intention.
This is the tarjih within the school.

Source Text

ثمَّ هذه النِّيَّة إنما تعتبر إذا وُجِدَتْ زمان حدوثِ سبب الملك، حتى لو نَوَى التجارة بعد حدوث سبب الملك لا تَجِبُ فيه الزَّكاةُ بنية التجارة، وهذا معنى قوله : ثمَّ لا يصير للتجارة، وإن نواه لها. ثُمَّ لا بُدَّ أن يكون سبب الملكِ سبباً اختيارياً، حتَّى لو نوى التجارة زمانَ تَمَلْكِهِ بالإرث لا تجب فيه الزكاة، ثُمَّ ذلك السبب الاختياري ، هل يجب أن يكون شراء أم لا ؟ فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لا يجب أن يكون شراء، بل كل عمل موجب للملك إذا اقترنت به نيَّةُ التِّجارة يكفي؛ لأنَّ التِّجارة عقد اكتساب المال، فما لا يدخل في ملكه إلا بقوله فهو كسبه، فيصح اقتران النية به. وعند محمد لا بد أن يكون شراء؛ لأنَّ ما سوى الشّراء من العقود كالهبة والوصية والصلح وغيرها لیست من عقود التجارة، ألا ترى أنَّ الإذن في التجارة لا يتضمن هذا العقود، ولا يملكها المضارب ولا العبد المأذون ، وهما يملكان التّصرف في عقود التجارة، فلا يعتبر اقترانُ النِّيَّة بهذه العقود. كذا في «البناية»، وذكر في «البدائع» و«البحر»: أنَّ قولَ محمد هو الأصح.
Then this intention is only considered if it exists at the time of occurrence of the cause of ownership, such that if one intended trade after the occurrence of the cause of ownership, zakat is not obligated on it by the intention of trade. This is the meaning of his statement: 'Then it does not become for trade, even if he intended it for it.' Then the cause of ownership must be a voluntary cause, such that if one intended trade at the time of his acquiring ownership by inheritance, zakat is not obligated on it. Then, regarding that voluntary cause, must it be purchase or not? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, it does not have to be purchase; rather, any action that causes ownership, if the intention of trade is connected with it, suffices, because trade is a contract for acquiring wealth, so whatever enters his ownership only by his statement is his acquisition, thus the connection of intention with it is valid. According to Muhammad, it must be purchase, because contracts other than purchase, such as gift, will, and compromise, are not contracts of trade. Do you not see that permission for trade does not include these contracts, nor does the mudarib or the authorized slave own them, while they are allowed to engage in contracts of trade. Thus, the connection of intention with these contracts is not considered. Thus in al-Binaya, and it is mentioned in al-Bada'i' and al-Bahr that Muhammad's statement is the most correct.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of intention (niyyah) for zakat on trade goods. The author explains that intention for trade must coincide with the cause of ownership. The cause must be voluntary (not inheritance). Then the dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that any voluntary acquisition (e.g., gift, will, compromise) suffices if accompanied by trade intention, because trade is a contract for acquiring wealth. Muhammad al-Shaybani restricts it to purchase, reasoning that other contracts are not part of trade dealings; a mudarib or authorized slave, who can trade, cannot engage in these contracts, so they are not considered trade acts. The author notes that in al-Bada'i' and al-Bahr, Muhammad's view is preferred (al-ashah). This reflects the Hanafi principle that intention qualifies the act, but the act must be inherently capable of being trade.

Key Terms
نية التجارة (niyyah al-tijārah): Intention to engage in trade (buying and selling for profit) when acquiring an asset.
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): The legal cause or transaction by which ownership is acquired (e.g., purchase, gift, inheritance).
سبب اختياري (sabab ikhtiyārī): A voluntary cause of ownership, one that depends on the individual's choice and action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only purchase
C) Any acquisition including inheritance
D) Only contracts involving exchange of money

Key Takeaway
For zakat on trade goods, intention must coincide with a voluntary acquisition; according to the mufta bihi view, only a purchase qualifies, not gifts, wills, or compromises.

Intention Required for Payment of Zakat
Stating ruling that zakat payment requires intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intention for zakat payment and explain the intra-madhhab difference on partial payment without intention.

Core Idea
Zakat payment requires intention; setting aside with intention suffices, and partial payment without intention is disputed among Hanafi imams.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses a fundamental question: can you pay zakat without intending it? The text presents the ruling, a key dispensation, and an internal disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute is essential for applying the correct fatwa in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling on intention
Read the first line: 'لا أداء إلا بنية' – there is no valid performance except with intention. Determine whether intention is a condition (shart) or a pillar (rukn).
In Hanafi fiqh, intention (niyyah) is a condition for acts of worship.
2
Understand the dispensation of setting aside (ʿazl)
The text allows intention to be made when setting aside the zakat amount, not necessarily at distribution. Note that this is a concession based on istihsan.
Setting aside with intention means the intention does not need to be repeated when giving.
3
Compare the two positions on partial payment
Abu Yusuf says partial payment without intention does not count. Muhammad says it counts for the portion given. Identify the practical difference.
The word 'الله' after Abu Yusuf is a likely misreading; the correct is 'لا' (no).
4
Analyze the qiyas argument
The text states that qiyas (analogy) requires intention because both the act and the obligation are legislated, so specification (taʿyīn) is needed. This is the view of Zufar and the three Imams.
The Hanafi school uses istihsan to allow setting aside, overriding qiyas.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi reliance
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that intention is required; the setting aside dispensation is accepted. For partial payment, the view of Muhammad is often preferred for ease.
The text does not explicitly choose, but later Hanafi authorities tend to follow Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أداء إلا بنيَّةٍ قُرنَت به ، أو بعزل قَدْرِ ما وَجَب، وتصدقُهُ بكلِّ مالِه بلا نية مسقط، وببعضه لا عند أبي يوسف الله ، وعند محمد له سقط زكاة المؤدى . قوله : مسقط؛ والقياس أن لا تسقط، وهو قول زفر له والأئمةُ الثَّلاثَةُ لأن الفعل والفرضَ كليهما مشروعان، فلا بد من نية التعيين.
And there is no valid performance except with intention connected to it, or by setting aside the amount that became obligatory. And his giving all his wealth without intention is nullifying, and giving some of it without intention is not valid according to Abu Yusuf, and according to Muhammad, the zakat of what was given is dropped. His statement: 'nullifying'; and the analogy is that it does not drop, and this is the view of Zufar and the three Imams, because the action and the obligation are both legislated, so intention of specification is necessary.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that the payment of zakat requires intention. The intention must accompany the act of payment or be made when setting aside (isolating) the obligatory amount. If one gives all his wealth without any intention, that does not fulfill zakat. If one gives only part of his wealth without intention, there is a dispute: Abu Yusuf says it does not count, while Muhammad says the zakat of that given amount is dropped (fulfilled). The text notes that the qiyas (analogy) suggests that without intention, zakat is not fulfilled, which is the position of Zufar and the three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad), because both the act of giving and the obligation are legislated acts requiring specification. The Hanafi position generally requires intention. The ruling that setting aside suffices is based on istihsan to avoid hardship.

Key Terms
عزل (ʿazl): Setting aside or isolating the zakat amount from one's wealth with intention.
نية التعيين (niyyah al-taʿyīn): Intention to specify that this payment is for zakat obligation.
مسقط (musqit): A term meaning 'nullifying' or 'causing the obligation to drop'; here it means the payment does not fulfill the obligation.
سقط زكاة المؤدى (saqata zakāt al-mu'addā): The zakat of the portion given is dropped (i.e., fulfilled).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) His zakat is fulfilled
B) His zakat is not fulfilled
C) It fulfills zakat only if it covers the required amount
D) It fulfills zakat only according to Muhammad

Key Takeaway
Zakat payment requires intention; setting aside with intention suffices, and partial payment without intention is acceptable according to Muhammad, the view often followed for fatwa.

Dispute on Zakat Fulfillment by Voluntary Charity without Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat fulfillment without intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether voluntary charity fulfills zakat without intention and explain the istihsan reasoning behind the preferred view.

Core Idea
If you give all your wealth in charity, zakat is waived – even without specifying it.

Imagine you give your entire savings to a needy person as a gift, without ever thinking about zakat. Did you just fulfill your zakat obligation? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement on this very question. This lesson unpacks the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf, the reasoning of istihsan, and which view is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Muhammad & Abu Hanifa)
Identify the ruling according to Muhammad: giving all wealth in charity without zakat intention drops the zakat; giving part drops the zakat on that part.
This is the view attributed to Abu Hanifa as well.
2
Identify the opponent's view (Abu Yusuf)
State Abu Yusuf's position: no zakat is dropped unless the intention of zakat is explicitly made.
He requires specification (ta'yin) for the discharge.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Abu Yusuf likely relies on the general requirement of intention for acts of worship, including zakat.
The passage does not elaborate his dalil, but it is implied.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response (istihsan)
The Hanafi response uses istihsan: the obligatory portion is a defined part of the wealth; when all is given in devotion, there is no competition among portions, so no specification is needed.
Analogous to fasting in Ramadan – any fast on those days suffices without specifying Ramadan.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: if someone gives all wealth without intention, according to Muhammad zakat is dropped, but according to Abu Yusuf, zakat remains due. Later authorities (Hidayah, Qadi Khan) prefer Muhammad's view.
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي إذا تصدق بجميع ماله بلا نيّة الزَّكاة تسقط الزَّكاة، وإن تصدق ببعض ماله تسقط زكاة المؤدى عند محمد له خلافاً لأبي يوسفه، حتى لو كان له مئتا درهم، فتصدق بمئة درهم، تسقط عند محمد الله زكاة المئة المؤداة ، وعند أبي يوسف لا تسقط عنه زكاة شيء أصلاً.
ولنا : وهو الاستحسان أنَّ الواجب جزء من جميع المال فكان معيناً فلا حاجة إلى التعيين ؛ لأنَّ التعيينَ إنَّما يشترط لمزاحمة سائر الأجزاء، فلما أدى الجميع على وجه القربة زالت المزاحمة فسقط الفرض، وهذا كالصوم في رمضان عندنا يصاب بمطلق الاسم. كذا في «البناية»(۱).
[١] قوله : بلا نيَّةِ الزَّكاة ؛ في هذا القيد مسامحة، فإنّه لو نوى بتصدق . جميع المال النذر أو الكفارة أو غيرهما يقعُ عما نوى ويضمنُ الزَّكاةَ مع أنه يصدق عليه أنه تصدق بلا نيَّة الزَّكاة، ولقد أحسن المصنِّفُ الله له حيث قال : بلا نيَّة ، على سبيل الإطلاق، ولم يتنبه الشارح له على دقيقة إطلاقه.
[٢]قوله : خلافاً لأبي يوسف ؛ أشار صاحب «الهدايــة»(۲)، و«ملتقـى الأبحر»، وقاضي خان في «فتاواه» إلى ترجيحه.
That is, if he gives in charity all his wealth without the intention of zakat, the zakat is dropped. And if he gives in charity some of his wealth, the zakat of the amount given is dropped according to Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. So if he had two hundred dirhams and gave in charity one hundred dirhams, according to Muhammad the zakat of the one hundred given is dropped, while according to Abu Yusuf no zakat is dropped from him at all. And for us: it is istihsan (juristic preference) that the obligation is a portion of the entire wealth, so it is specified and there is no need for specification (of intention), because specification is only required due to the competition of other portions. When he pays all of it in the manner of devotion, the competition is removed and the obligation is dropped. This is like fasting in Ramadan according to us, which is achieved by the absolute name (of fasting). Thus in al-Binaya (1). [1] His saying: 'without the intention of zakat' – in this qualification there is a leniency, because if he intended with his charity of all his wealth a vow or expiation or other, it falls for what he intended and he guarantees zakat even though it is true that he gave charity without the intention of zakat. The author has done well in saying 'without intention' in an absolute sense, and the commentator did not pay attention to the precision of his absoluteness. [2] His saying: 'contrary to Abu Yusuf' – the author of al-Hidayah, Multaqa al-Abhar, and Qadi Khan in his Fatawa indicated its preference.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether voluntary charity (sadaqah) can fulfill the obligation of zakat if given without the specific intention of zakat. The text first states the ruling: if someone gives all his wealth in charity without intending zakat, the zakat obligation is dropped. If he gives only part of his wealth, then according to Imam Muhammad, the zakat on that portion is dropped, but according to Abu Yusuf, none of the zakat is dropped at all. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) relies on istihsan: since the obligatory zakat is a specific portion of the total wealth, when the entire wealth is given away in devotion, the obligation is fulfilled without requiring explicit intention. This is analogous to fasting in Ramadan, where merely fasting on those days suffices without specifying the intention for Ramadan. The super-commentary then refines the wording, noting that the author's phrase 'without intention of zakat' is meant absolutely, so that even if one intended a different act of worship like a vow, the zakat is still not fulfilled; he must also pay zakat. The commentary also notes that later authorities like al-Hidayah and Qadi Khan preferred Muhammad's position.

Key Terms
تعيين (ta'yin): Explicitly specifying the intention of zakat when giving charity.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to override strict analogy; here, it allows the charity to count as zakat even without intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person gives only part of his wealth in charity without intending zakat, what is the ruling according to Muhammad?
A) Zakat on the entire wealth is dropped.
B) Zakat on the given portion is dropped.
C) No zakat is dropped at all.
D) Zakat is dropped only if he also intends zakat.

Key Takeaway
When all wealth is given in charity, zakat is discharged without needing to specify it, due to istihsan; partial charity drops zakat on that portion only according to the preferred view.

Nisab for Livestock: Camels, Cows, and Sheep
Definition of nisab for livestock with hadith proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab thresholds for camels, cows, and sheep in Hanafi fiqh and cite the hadith proofs for each.

Core Idea
Zakat on livestock begins at 5 camels, 30 cows, or 40 sheep that graze freely.

Zakat on livestock is a fundamental obligation in Islam, but it is not due until a minimum threshold (nisab) is reached. This lesson presents the nisab amounts for camels, cows, and sheep as established by authentic hadith and scholarly consensus. We will also examine the condition that the animals must be grazing (sa'imah) and learn that the ruling applies to both one-humped and two-humped camels.

Guided Path
1
Identify the livestock types and their nisab thresholds
The text specifies nisab for three types: camels (5), cows (30), and sheep (40). Note that the nisab is the minimum number—anything below is exempt.
The nisab for cows is agreed upon by all scholars.
2
Recognize the condition 'sa'imah'
The animals must be 'sa'imah'—grazing freely for most of the year. If they are fed by the owner, zakat may not be due even if the number is met.
This condition is mentioned in the matn and is critical for obligation.
3
Examine the hadith proofs for each nisab
For camels: 'There is no charity on less than five dhud.' For cows: the hadith of Mu'adh. For sheep: 'In every forty sheep, one sheep.' Note that the hadiths are from reliable sources.
Al-Zayla'i confirms consensus on the cows nisab.
4
Understand the inclusion of both camel types
The text mentions 'bakt' (Bactrian) and 'arab' (Arabian) to show that the ruling covers all camels, as the Quranic term 'ibl' is comprehensive.
No distinction in zakat based on camel breed.
5
Apply the nisab to a practical scenario
Try calculating zakat for 25 camels: the nisab is 5, so zakat is due. The rates vary as numbers increase, but this lesson focuses on the starting point.
Further rates (e.g., 10 camels, 15 camels) are covered in other lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زكاة الأموال
نصاب الإبل خمس، والبقر ثلاثون، والغنم أربعون سائمة، وفي كل خمس من الإبل بخت أو عراب : شاة
[1] باب زكاة الأموال
(نصاب الإبل خمس ، والبقر ثلاثون، والغنم أربعون سائمة. وفي كل خمس من الإبل بخت) أو عراب : شاة
[۱] قوله : خمس ؛ فلا يجب بشيء فيما دون الخمس ؛ لحديث: «ليس فيما دون خمسة ذود صدقة ، والمراد بالذود : الإبل ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما. [٢]قوله : والبقر ثلاثون ؛ لحديث معاذ الله : «إنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم لما وجَهَهُ إِلى اليمنِ أَمَرَهُ أن يأخذ من كل ثلاثين بقرة تبيعاً أو تبيعة» (۲) ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن الأربعة وغيرهم، قال الزيلعي (۳) : لا خلاف بين العلماء في أنَّ السُنة في زكاة البقر ما في حديث معاذ ، وأنه النصاب المجمع عليه فيها». (۳) قوله : أربعون ؛ لحديث: «في الغنم في كل أربعين شاة شاة، فإن لم يكن إلا تسعة وثلثون فليس عليك فيها شيء» (٤) ، أخرجه أبو داود، وغيره. (0) قوله : بخت ؛ بالضم جمع بختي، وهو ما لَهُ سنامان، منسوب الى بخت نصر ؛ لأنه أوَّلُ مَن جمع بين العربي والعجمي.
[٦]قوله : أو عراب ؛ بالكسر ، وجمع عربي وهو يطلقُ مقابل للبختي، وإنما ذكر هذين القسمين لكونهما أشهر أصنافِ الإبل، وإشارة إلى أن الحكم غير مختص بأحد القسمين بل يعمهما ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الحكم وردَ في الشَّرع بلفظ الإبل، وهو نوع شامل
Chapter of Zakat on Wealth. The nisab of camels is five, of cows thirty, of sheep forty, all grazing livestock. And for every five camels, whether Bakt or Arab, one sheep. [1] Chapter of Zakat on Wealth. (The nisab of camels is five, of cows thirty, of sheep forty, grazing. And for every five camels, Bakt) or Arab, one sheep. [1] His saying: 'five' – so nothing is obligatory on less than five due to the hadith: 'There is no charity on less than five dhud,' and dhud means camels, related by Bukhari and Muslim and others. [2] His saying: 'and cows thirty' – due to the hadith of Mu'adh: 'The Prophet, when he sent him to Yemen, ordered him to take from every thirty cows a tabi' or a tabi'ah,' related by the authors of the four Sunans and others. Al-Zayla'i said: 'There is no disagreement among scholars that the sunnah in zakat on cows is what is in the hadith of Mu'adh, and that it is the nisab agreed upon.' [3] His saying: 'forty' – due to the hadith: 'In sheep, for every forty sheep one sheep; if there are only thirty-nine, there is nothing on you,' related by Abu Dawud and others. (5) His saying: 'Bakt' – with damma, plural of Bakhti, which is one with two humps, attributed to Bukht Nasr because he was the first to breed between Arabian and non-Arabian. [6] His saying: 'or Arab' – with kasra, plural of Arabi, which is the opposite of Bakhti. These two types are mentioned because they are the most famous kinds of camels, and to indicate that the ruling is not specific to one type but includes both, because the ruling came in the Shari'ah with the word 'ibl' (camels), which is a comprehensive term.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on zakat of wealth (specifically livestock). It states the nisab thresholds: for camels, 5; for cows, 30; for sheep, 40, all must be grazing (sa'imah). The matn also states that for every five camels, one sheep is due, whether the camel is of the Bakt (two-humped) or Arab (one-humped) variety. The sharh then provides hadith proofs for each nisab. For camels, the hadith 'not less than five camels' is cited. For cows, the well-known hadith of Mu'adh is presented, and al-Zayla'i confirms scholarly consensus on this nisab. For sheep, the hadith 'for every forty sheep, one sheep' is given. The sharh then defines Bakt as the two-humped camel, and Arab as the one-humped, noting that the ruling applies to both, as the term 'ibl' in the Shari'ah encompasses all types.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisab): The minimum threshold of wealth that makes zakat obligatory.
سائمة (sa'imah): Grazing livestock that are left to pasture most of the year, not fed by the owner.
بخت (bakt): Two-humped camel (Bactrian camel).
عراب (arab): One-humped camel (Arabian camel).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nisab for cows in zakat?
A) 5
B) 30
C) 40
D) 200

Key Takeaway
The nisab for livestock zakat is 5 camels, 30 cows, or 40 sheep that are sa'imah, established by authentic hadith and consensus.

Zakat on Livestock: Nisab and Camel Age Categories
Presentation of nisab thresholds and camel zakat schedule with hadith proofs and age definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab for camels, cows, and sheep, calculate the zakat due on a given number of camels using the Hanafi schedule, and explain the age categories (bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqah, jadha'ah).

Core Idea
Zakat on livestock is triggered at specific nisab thresholds, and the camel schedule requires graduated age-based payments from 25 camels upward.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational nisab thresholds for camels, cows, and sheep as prescribed in the Hanafi school. We also delve into the detailed camel zakat schedule, which distinguishes between different age categories of camels, ensuring that the obligation is fair and manageable for the owner. The passage provides hadith proofs for each nisab and explains the wisdom behind obligating young female camels.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab thresholds
The passage states the nisab for camels is 5, cows 30, and sheep 40, all conditioned on the animals being sa'imah (free-grazing). This is the minimum number that triggers zakat.
Sa'imah means livestock that grazes freely for most of the year; if hand-fed, zakat may not be due in some cases.
2
Understand the hadith proofs for each nisab
For camels, the hadith 'There is no sadaqah for less than five dhoud' establishes the nisab. For cows, the hadith of Mu'adh sets the nisab at thirty, and al-Zayla'i confirms consensus. For sheep, the hadith 'for every forty sheep, one sheep' sets the nisab at forty.
Cows' nisab is confirmed by consensus (ijma), making it particularly strong evidence.
3
Analyze the camel zakat schedule from 5 to 120
For 5–24 camels, the zakat is one sheep per 5 camels. From 25 camels onward, specific age categories apply: 25–35 → 1 bint makhad (1yr); 36–45 → 1 bint labun (2yr); 46–60 → 1 hiqqah (3yr); 61–75 → 1 jadha'ah (4yr); 76–90 → 2 bint labun; 91–120 → 2 hiqqah.
The schedule is designed to be easy on the owner, obligating younger females which are less valuable for sacrifice but still fulfilling the obligation.
4
Define the age categories
Bint makhad is a she-camel in its second year (1–2 years old). Bint labun is in its third year (2–3). Hiqqah is in its fourth year (3–4). Jadha'ah is in its fifth year (4–5). These definitions are crucial for correct zakat calculation.
The names derive from the camel's life stage: bint makhad means 'daughter of a pregnant camel' because its mother is often pregnant again.
5
Appreciate the generality of 'ibil' covering both Bikht and 'Arab
The ruling applies to all types of camels—both Bikht (two-humped) and 'Arab (one-humped)—because the Shari'ah uses the general term 'ibil'. Similarly, rulings for sheep and cows cover all their varieties.
This illustrates a key usul principle: when the Shari'ah uses a general term, the ruling applies to all instances unless evidence specifies otherwise.

Chapter of Zakat of Wealth: The nisab of camels is five, of cows is thirty, and of sheep is forty, provided they are sa'imah. For every five camels, whether Bikht or Arab, one sheep. [1] He said: five; so nothing is obligatory for less than five, due to the hadith: 'There is no sadaqah for less than five dhoud,' and dhoud means camels, narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, and others. [2] He said: and cows are thirty, due to the hadith of Mu'adh: 'When the Prophet sent him to Yemen, he ordered him to take from every thirty cows a tabi' or tabi'ah,' narrated by the four Sunan and others. Al-Zayla'i said: There is no disagreement among scholars that the sunnah in zakat of cows is what is in the hadith of Mu'adh and that it is the nisab upon which there is consensus. [3] He said: forty, due to the hadith: 'For every forty sheep, one sheep; if there are only thirty-nine, nothing is due on you,' narrated by Abu Dawud and others. [0] He said: Bikht (with damma) plural of Bikhtiyy, which has two humps, attributed to Bukht Nasr because he was the first to cross the Arabian and non-Arabian. [6] He said: or 'Arab (with kasr) plural of 'Arabiyy, the opposite of Bikhtiyy. These two types are mentioned because they are the most famous types of camels, indicating that the ruling is not specific to one type but includes them both, because the ruling came in the Shari'ah with the word 'ibil' which is a general type including Bikht and 'Arab, so the ruling includes both unless evidence indicates specificity for one. This is like the generality of the ruling that came for sheep in all its types, and the ruling for cows in all its types. Apply analogy to what is appropriate. Then for every twenty-five (camels), a bint makhad. Then for thirty-six, a bint labun. Then for forty-six, a hiqqah. Then for sixty-one, a jadha'ah. Then for seventy-six, two bint labun. Then for ninety-one, two hiqqah, up to one hundred and twenty. [He said:] for every twenty-five, bint makhad; for thirty-six, bint labun; for forty-six, hiqqah; for sixty-one, jadha'ah; for seventy-six, two bint labun; for ninety-one, two hiqqah, up to one hundred and twenty. For Bikht and 'Arab, so the ruling includes both unless evidence indicates specificity. [1] He said: Bint makhad is the she-camel that has completed one year and entered the second year; so named because its mother is pregnant with another. It is said 'makhad' meaning the pain of childbirth. [2] He said: Bint labun (with fatha on lam) is she that has completed two years and started the third; so named because its mother often has milk for another. [3] He said: Hiqqah (with kasr of ha and tashdid of qaf) is she that has completed three years and entered the fourth; so named because it is more deserving of being ridden. [4] He said: Jadha'ah (with fatha) is she that has entered the fifth year; so named because it cuts its milk teeth. [0] He said: Two bint labun; al-'Ayni said in al-Binayah: The Shari'ah made the obligation in the nisab of camels the young rather than the old because they are not valid for sacrifice, and this was chosen to make it easier for livestock owners. Also, the obligation is female, not male, so that the male cannot be given except with value, because femaleness is considered superior.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on Zakat of Wealth. It opens by stating the nisab (minimum threshold) for zakat on three types of livestock: camels (5), cows (30), and sheep (40), provided they are sa'imah (grazing freely for most of the year). For every 5 camels, one sheep is due, regardless of whether the camels are Bikht (two-humped, from Central Asia) or 'Arab (one-humped, Arabian). The commentary then provides hadith proofs for each nisab. For camels, the hadith 'There is no sadaqah for less than five dhoud' establishes the nisab of five. For cows, the hadith of Mu'adh (narrated by the four Sunan) sets the nisab at thirty, and al-Zayla'i confirms consensus among scholars on this point. For sheep, the hadith 'for every forty sheep, one sheep' sets the nisab at forty. Then the commentary explains the terms Bikht and 'Arab, emphasizing that the ruling applies to both types because the Shari'ah uses the general term 'ibil' (camels). Next, the text details the camel zakat schedule from 25 to 120 camels: at 25-35, one bint makhad (1-year-old); at 36-45, one bint labun (2-year-old); at 46-60, one hiqqah (3-year-old); at 61-75, one jadha'ah (4-year-old); at 76-90, two bint labun; at 91-120, two hiqqah. The commentary then defines each age category: bint makhad is a she-camel in its second year; bint labun in its third year; hiqqah in its fourth year; jadha'ah in its fifth year. Finally, al-'Ayni explains the wisdom behind obligating young female camels: they are easier on owners (as they are not used for sacrifice) and females are preferable due to their reproductive value.

Key Terms
نصاب (nisāb): The minimum quantity of wealth that triggers the obligation of zakat.
سائمة (sā'imah): Livestock that grazes freely in pasture for most of the year.
بخت (bikht): A type of camel with two humps, also called Bactrian camel.
عراب (‘arāb): A type of camel with one hump, also called Arabian camel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Zakat on livestock is fard on sa'imah animals when the nisab is reached; the camel schedule requires careful attention to age categories from 25 onward, and the ruling applies to all camel types due to the generality of the term 'ibil'.

Camel Zakah Schedule: From 5 to 120 Camels
Ruling with proof and terminological explanations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory zakah schedule for camels from 5 to 120 and identify the age category required for each nisab band.

Core Idea
The obligatory zakah schedule for camels up to 120 is agreed upon and precisely defined by age categories.

Zakah on camels is a pillar of wealth purification. The Prophet ﷺ prescribed a detailed schedule based on the number of camels owned. In this lesson, you will learn the exact obligations for each nisab band from 5 to 120 camels, including the definitions of the required animals: bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqah, and jadha'ah. The schedule is supported by authentic hadith and consensus of the majority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab bands
Memorize the thresholds: every 5 camels from 5 to 24 give a sheep; at 25: bint makhad; 36: bint labun; 46: hiqqah; 61: jadha'ah; 76: two bint labun; 91: two hiqqahs up to 120.
This is the agreed-upon schedule from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Understand the definitions
Learn the age and naming rationale for bint makhad (1-year), bint labun (2-year), hiqqah (3-year), and jadha'ah (4-year).
These terms are specific to camel zakah and are not disputed.
3
Note the scope
The schedule applies to both arabi and bukhti camels unless evidence specifies otherwise.
This is like the generality for sheep and cattle.
4
Recognize the wisdom
The Lawgiver required young females (not old or males) for ease on owners and because old camels are reserved for sacrifice.
Al-Ayni explains this in al-Binaya.
5
Confirm the evidence
The schedule is proven by authentic hadith from multiple Companions, recorded in the major hadith collections.
It is mutafaq alayh among the majority.

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
ثم في كل خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض. ثم في ست وثلاثين بنت لبون. ثم في ست وأربعين حقه. ثم في إحدى وستين جذعة. ثم في ست وسبعين بنتا لبون. ثم في إحدى وتسعين حقتان إلى مئة وعشرين.
للبختي والعربي، فيشمل الحكم كليهما إلا أن يدل دليل على اختصاصه بأحدهما، وهذا كعموم الحكم الوارد في الشاة لجميع أقسامها، والوارد في البقر لجميع أصنافه، وقس عليه ما يناسبه.
قوله: بنت مخاض؛ هي الناقة التي تمت لها سنة، ودخلت في السنة الثانية، سميت بها لأن أمها حملت بغيرها، يقال محضت الحامل مخاضاً، بالفتح: أي أخذها وجع الولادة.
قوله: بنت لبون؛ بفتح اللام: هي التي تمت لها سنتان، وشرعت في الثالثة؛ سميت به لأن أمها تكون ذات لبن لأخرى غالباً.
قوله: حقة، بكسر الحاء المهملة، وتشديد القاف: هي التي تم لها ثلاث سنين، ودخلت في الرابعة، سميت به لكونها أحق أن يركب عليها.
قوله: جذعة؛ بفتحات: هي التي دخلت في السنة الخامسة، سميت به لأنها تجذع: أي تقلع أسنان اللبن.
قوله: بنتا لبون؛ قال العيني في «البناية»: إن الشرع جعل الواجب في نصاب الإبل الصغار دون الكبار لسبب أن الأضحية لا تجوز بها، وإنما اختار ذلك تيسيراً على أرباب المواشي، وجعل أيضاً الواجب الإناث لا الذكور؛ حتى لا يجوز دفع الذكر إلا بالقيمة؛ لأن الأنوثة تعد فضلاً.
كتاب الزكاة زكاة الأموال
ثم في كل خمس شاة
قوله: ثم في كل خمس... الخ؛ وجوب ما ذكر إلى مئة وعشرين من الشاة في كل خمس وبنت مخاض من خمس وعشرين، وبنت لبون من ست وثلاثين، وحقة من ست وأربعين، وجذعة من إحدى وستين وبنتي لبون في ست وأربعين، وحقتين من إحدى وتسعين إلى مئة وعشرين، متفق عليه بين الجمهور وثابت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية جمع من الصحابة المخرجة في «صحيح البخاري»، و«جامع الترمذي»، وسنن أبي داود و«النسائي» وغيرهم.
واختلف فيما بعد المئة والعشرين: فذهب الشافعي رحمه الله وغيره إلى أنه لا استئناف هاهنا، بل يجب بعده في كل أربعين بنت لبون، وفي كل خمسين حقة، وبه ورد التصريح في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره.
(١) فعن ابن عمر: إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كتب كتاب الصدقة، فلم يخرجه إلى عماله حتى قبض، فقرنه بسيفه، فلما قبض عمل به أبو بكر حتى قبض، وعمر حتى قبض، وكان فيه: في خمس من الإبل شاة، وفي عشر شاتان، وفي خمس عشر ثلاث شياه، وفي عشرين أربع شياه، وفي خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض إلى خمس وثلاثين، فإذا زادت ففيها ابنة لبون إلى خمس وأربعين، فإذا زادت ففيها حقة إلى ستين، فإذا زادت فجذعة إلى خمس وسبعين، فإذا زادت ففيها ابنتا لبون إلى تسعين، فإذا زادت ففيها حقتان إلى عشرين ومئة، فإذا زادت على عشرين ومئة، ففي كل خمسين حقة، وفي كل أربعين ابنة لبون. رواه الترمذي وحسنه، والمستدرك، وأبو داود.
(٢) ففي كتاب أبي بكر له: في أربع وعشرين من الإبل فما دونها من الغنم من كل خمس، فإذا بلغت خمساً وعشرين إلى خمس وثلاثين ففيها بنت مخاض أنثى، فإذا بلغت ستاً وثلاثين إلى خمس وأربعين ففيها بنت لبون أنثى، فإذا بلغت ستاً وأربعين إلى ستين ففيها حقة طروقة الجمل، فإذا بلغت واحداً وستين إلى خمس وسبعين ففيها جذعة، فإذا بلغت ستاً وسبعين إلى تسعين ففيها بنتا لبون، فإذا بلغت إحدى وتسعين إلى عشرين ومئة ففيها حقتان طروقتا الجمل، فإذا زادت على عشرين ومئة، ففي كل أربعين بنت لبون، وفي كل خمسين حقة، ومن لم يكن معه إلا أربع من الإبل فليس فيها صدقة إلا أن يشاء ربها، فإذا بلغت خمساً من الإبل ففيها شاة. رواه البخاري.
يعني شاة.
Then for every twenty-five (camels), a bint makhad. Then for thirty-six, a bint labun. Then for forty-six, a hiqqah. Then for sixty-one, a jadha'ah. Then for seventy-six, two bint labun. Then for ninety-one, two hiqqahs up to one hundred and twenty. For both the bukhti and the arabi (types of camels), the ruling includes both unless evidence indicates specificity to one. This is like the generality of the ruling regarding sheep for all its types, and regarding cattle for all its kinds. Measure accordingly.
His saying: bint makhad: it is the she-camel that has completed one year and entered the second year. It is named thus because its mother has conceived another. One says 'mahadat al-hamil makhadan' meaning the pains of childbirth seized her.
His saying: bint labun (with fatha on the lam): it is the one that has completed two years and entered the third; named thus because its mother is usually lactating for another.
His saying: hiqqah (with kasr on the ha and tashdid on the qaf): it is the one that has completed three years and entered the fourth; named thus because it is more deserving to be ridden.
His saying: jadha'ah (with fatha on all letters): it is the one that has entered the fifth year; named thus because it sheds its milk teeth.
His saying: two bint labun. al-Ayni in al-Binaya said: The Lawgiver made the obligatory (zakah) in the nisab of camels from the young rather than the old because the old are not valid for sacrifice, and this was chosen for ease upon the owners. He also made the obligatory from females, not males, so that a male cannot be given except by value, because being female is considered a virtue.
The Book of Zakah: Zakah of Wealth
Then for every five (camels), a sheep.
His saying: 'then for every five...' etc.: The obligation of what was mentioned up to one hundred and twenty: a sheep for every five, a bint makhad from twenty-five, a bint labun from thirty-six, a hiqqah from forty-six, a jadha'ah from sixty-one, two bint labun from seventy-six, and two hiqqahs from ninety-one up to one hundred and twenty. This is agreed upon by the majority and established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) through a narration from a group of Companions, recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sunan Abi Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and others.
They differed regarding what is beyond one hundred and twenty: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) and others held that there is no restart here, but that after it, for every forty (camels) a bint labun, and for every fifty a hiqqah, and this was explicitly stated in Sahih al-Bukhari and others.
(1) From Ibn 'Umar: Indeed the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote the Book of Charity, but did not send it to his governors until he passed away. He kept it with his sword. When he passed away, Abu Bakr acted upon it until he died, and then 'Umar until he died. In it was: for five camels, a sheep; for ten, two sheep; for fifteen, three sheep; for twenty, four sheep; for twenty-five to thirty-five, a bint makhad; when it exceeds, then a bint labun to forty-five; when it exceeds, then a hiqqah to sixty; when it exceeds, then a jadha'ah to seventy-five; when it exceeds, then two bint labun to ninety; when it exceeds, then two hiqqahs to one hundred and twenty; when it exceeds one hundred and twenty, then for every fifty a hiqqah, and for every forty a bint labun. Reported by al-Tirmidhi and he considered it hasan, and al-Mustadrak, and Abu Dawud.
(2) In the letter of Abu Bakr to him: For twenty-four camels and below, from sheep for every five. When it reaches twenty-five to thirty-five, then a bint makhad (female). When it reaches thirty-six to forty-five, then a bint labun (female). When it reaches forty-six to sixty, then a hiqqah that is mountable by the camel. When it reaches sixty-one to seventy-five, then a jadha'ah. When it reaches seventy-six to ninety, then two bint labun. When it reaches ninety-one to one hundred twenty, then two hiqqahs mountable by the camel. When it exceeds one hundred twenty, then for every forty a bint labun, and for every fifty a hiqqah. And whoever has only four camels, there is no charity on them unless the owner wishes. When it reaches five camels, then a sheep. Reported by al-Bukhari and others.
Meaning: a sheep.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the obligatory zakah schedule for camels in the Hanafi school. The matn (text) presents the schedule in a concise form: for each nisab interval (5, 25, 36, 46, 61, 76, 91 up to 120), a specific animal must be given. The commentary then clarifies that this schedule applies to both types of camels (bukhti and arabi) unless evidence specifies otherwise, paralleling the general rulings for sheep and cattle.

The commentary defines the four key age categories:
- Bint makhad: a one-year-old she-camel (entering second year), named because its mother is pregnant with another.
- Bint labun: a two-year-old she-camel (entering third year), named because its mother is usually lactating for another calf.
- Hiqqah: a three-year-old she-camel (entering fourth year), named because it is fit to be ridden.
- Jadha'ah: a four-year-old she-camel (entering fifth year), named because it sheds its milk teeth.

The note from al-Ayni explains the wisdom behind requiring young females rather than old or male camels: young camels are not valid for sacrifice (thus preserving sacrificial animals) and it is easier on the owners; females are preferred due to their reproductive value.

The passage then reaches the matn phrase 'then for every five (camels), a sheep' (meaning from 5 to 24 camels, the obligation is a sheep for every 5). The commentary summarizes the entire schedule up to 120 camels, confirming it is agreed upon by the majority (jumhur) and proven by multiple authentic hadith narrated by a group of Companions.

Finally, a dispute (ikhtilaf) is noted regarding camels beyond 120. Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) held that the schedule does not restart; instead, for every 40 camels one gives a bint labun, and for every 50 a hiqqah. This view is supported by explicit texts in Sahih al-Bukhari and others. The text does not explicitly state the Hanafi position here, but the context implies that the Hanafi school holds that the schedule restarts from 5 after 120 (as indicated by the matn ending at 120). The passage provides two hadith proofs: the letter of Abu Bakr (as transmitted by Ibn 'Umar) and the similar letter of Abu Bakr recorded by al-Bukhari, both of which include the schedule up to 120 and then mention the 40/50 rule, supporting the Shafi'i view. The Hanafi position relies on a different interpretation or another hadith, but this is not elaborated in this passage.

Key Terms
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A she-camel that has completed one year and entered the second year; named because its mother is pregnant (makhd) with another.
بنت لبون (bint labun): A she-camel that has completed two years and entered the third; named because its mother is usually lactating.
حقة (hiqqah): A she-camel that has completed three years and entered the fourth; named because it is fit (ahaqq) to be ridden.
جذعة (jadha'ah): A she-camel that has entered the fifth year; named because it sheds its milk teeth.

Camel Zakah Beyond 120: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute with hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions regarding zakah on camels exceeding 120 and identify the evidence each school uses.

Core Idea
After 120 camels, Hanafis restart the schedule while Shafi'is apply a 40/50 rule – a key difference based on hadith interpretation.

The zakah schedule up to 120 camels is universally agreed. However, beyond that, a notable ikhtilaf arises. Imam al-Shafi'i and others hold that for every 40 camels a bint labun and for every 50 a hiqqah is due, citing explicit hadith texts. The Hanafi school, as implied in this passage, holds that the schedule restarts from 5. In this lesson, we explore both positions, their proofs, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of divergence
After 120 camels, does the schedule restart or continue with a new rule?
The ikhtilaf begins exactly at 121.
2
State the Shafi'i position
For every 40 camels, a bint labun; for every 50, a hiqqah.
This is based on explicit hadith texts.
3
Examine the Shafi'i evidence
The hadith from Ibn 'Umar and Abu Bakr's letter include the phrase 'for every fifty a hiqqah and for every forty a bint labun' after 120.
These are recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and other authentic collections.
4
Understand the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that after 120, the schedule restarts from the beginning: 5 camels yield a sheep, 10 two sheep, etc.
This is the implied position from the matn ending at 120, though not explicitly argued here.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
For 130 camels, the Hanafi obligation includes animals from the restarted schedule, while the Shafi'i obligation uses the 40/50 rule, leading to different amounts.
This practical difference matters for actual zakah calculation.

Source Text

عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية
ثم في كل خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض. ثم في ست وثلاثين بنت لبون. ثم في ست وأربعين حقه. ثم في إحدى وستين جذعة. ثم في ست وسبعين بنتا لبون. ثم في إحدى وتسعين حقتان إلى مئة وعشرين.
للبختي والعربي، فيشمل الحكم كليهما إلا أن يدل دليل على اختصاصه بأحدهما، وهذا كعموم الحكم الوارد في الشاة لجميع أقسامها، والوارد في البقر لجميع أصنافه، وقس عليه ما يناسبه.
قوله: بنت مخاض؛ هي الناقة التي تمت لها سنة، ودخلت في السنة الثانية، سميت بها لأن أمها حملت بغيرها، يقال محضت الحامل مخاضاً، بالفتح: أي أخذها وجع الولادة.
قوله: بنت لبون؛ بفتح اللام: هي التي تمت لها سنتان، وشرعت في الثالثة؛ سميت به لأن أمها تكون ذات لبن لأخرى غالباً.
قوله: حقة، بكسر الحاء المهملة، وتشديد القاف: هي التي تم لها ثلاث سنين، ودخلت في الرابعة، سميت به لكونها أحق أن يركب عليها.
قوله: جذعة؛ بفتحات: هي التي دخلت في السنة الخامسة، سميت به لأنها تجذع: أي تقلع أسنان اللبن.
قوله: بنتا لبون؛ قال العيني في «البناية»: إن الشرع جعل الواجب في نصاب الإبل الصغار دون الكبار لسبب أن الأضحية لا تجوز بها، وإنما اختار ذلك تيسيراً على أرباب المواشي، وجعل أيضاً الواجب الإناث لا الذكور؛ حتى لا يجوز دفع الذكر إلا بالقيمة؛ لأن الأنوثة تعد فضلاً.
كتاب الزكاة زكاة الأموال
ثم في كل خمس شاة
قوله: ثم في كل خمس... الخ؛ وجوب ما ذكر إلى مئة وعشرين من الشاة في كل خمس وبنت مخاض من خمس وعشرين، وبنت لبون من ست وثلاثين، وحقة من ست وأربعين، وجذعة من إحدى وستين وبنتي لبون في ست وأربعين، وحقتين من إحدى وتسعين إلى مئة وعشرين، متفق عليه بين الجمهور وثابت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية جمع من الصحابة المخرجة في «صحيح البخاري»، و«جامع الترمذي»، وسنن أبي داود و«النسائي» وغيرهم.
واختلف فيما بعد المئة والعشرين: فذهب الشافعي رحمه الله وغيره إلى أنه لا استئناف هاهنا، بل يجب بعده في كل أربعين بنت لبون، وفي كل خمسين حقة، وبه ورد التصريح في «صحيح البخاري» وغيره.
(١) فعن ابن عمر: إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كتب كتاب الصدقة، فلم يخرجه إلى عماله حتى قبض، فقرنه بسيفه، فلما قبض عمل به أبو بكر حتى قبض، وعمر حتى قبض، وكان فيه: في خمس من الإبل شاة، وفي عشر شاتان، وفي خمس عشر ثلاث شياه، وفي عشرين أربع شياه، وفي خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض إلى خمس وثلاثين، فإذا زادت ففيها ابنة لبون إلى خمس وأربعين، فإذا زادت ففيها حقة إلى ستين، فإذا زادت فجذعة إلى خمس وسبعين، فإذا زادت ففيها ابنتا لبون إلى تسعين، فإذا زادت ففيها حقتان إلى عشرين ومئة، فإذا زادت على عشرين ومئة، ففي كل خمسين حقة، وفي كل أربعين ابنة لبون. رواه الترمذي وحسنه، والمستدرك، وأبو داود.
(٢) ففي كتاب أبي بكر له: في أربع وعشرين من الإبل فما دونها من الغنم من كل خمس، فإذا بلغت خمساً وعشرين إلى خمس وثلاثين ففيها بنت مخاض أنثى، فإذا بلغت ستاً وثلاثين إلى خمس وأربعين ففيها بنت لبون أنثى، فإذا بلغت ستاً وأربعين إلى ستين ففيها حقة طروقة الجمل، فإذا بلغت واحداً وستين إلى خمس وسبعين ففيها جذعة، فإذا بلغت ستاً وسبعين إلى تسعين ففيها بنتا لبون، فإذا بلغت إحدى وتسعين إلى عشرين ومئة ففيها حقتان طروقتا الجمل، فإذا زادت على عشرين ومئة، ففي كل أربعين بنت لبون، وفي كل خمسين حقة، ومن لم يكن معه إلا أربع من الإبل فليس فيها صدقة إلا أن يشاء ربها، فإذا بلغت خمساً من الإبل ففيها شاة. رواه البخاري.
يعني شاة.
Then for every twenty-five (camels), a bint makhad. Then for thirty-six, a bint labun. Then for forty-six, a hiqqah. Then for sixty-one, a jadha'ah. Then for seventy-six, two bint labun. Then for ninety-one, two hiqqahs up to one hundred and twenty. For both the bukhti and the arabi (types of camels), the ruling includes both unless evidence indicates specificity to one. This is like the generality of the ruling regarding sheep for all its types, and regarding cattle for all its kinds. Measure accordingly.
His saying: bint makhad: it is the she-camel that has completed one year and entered the second year. It is named thus because its mother has conceived another. One says 'mahadat al-hamil makhadan' meaning the pains of childbirth seized her.
His saying: bint labun (with fatha on the lam): it is the one that has completed two years and entered the third; named thus because its mother is usually lactating for another.
His saying: hiqqah (with kasr on the ha and tashdid on the qaf): it is the one that has completed three years and entered the fourth; named thus because it is more deserving to be ridden.

The Book of Zakah: Zakah of Wealth
Then for every five (camels), a sheep.
His saying: 'then for every five...' etc.: The obligation of what was mentioned up to one hundred and twenty: a sheep for every five, a bint makhad from twenty-five, a bint labun from thirty-six, a hiqqah from forty-six, a jadha'ah from sixty-one, two bint labun from seventy-six, and two hiqqahs from ninety-one up to one hundred and twenty. This is agreed upon by the majority and established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) through a narration from a group of Companions, recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sunan Abi Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and others.
They differed regarding what is beyond one hundred and twenty: al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) and others held that there is no restart here, but that after it, for every forty (camels) a bint labun, and for every fifty a hiqqah, and this was explicitly stated in Sahih al-Bukhari and others.
(1) From Ibn 'Umar: Indeed the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote the Book of Charity, but did not send it to his governors until he passed away. He kept it with his sword. When he passed away, Abu Bakr acted upon it until he died, and then 'Umar until he died. In it was: for five camels, a sheep; for ten, two sheep; for fifteen, three sheep; for twenty, four sheep; for twenty-five to thirty-five, a bint makhad; when it exceeds, then a bint labun to forty-five; when it exceeds, then a hiqqah to sixty; when it exceeds, then a jadha'ah to seventy-five; when it exceeds, then two bint labun to ninety; when it exceeds, then two hiqqahs to one hundred and twenty; when it exceeds one hundred and twenty, then for every fifty a hiqqah, and for every forty a bint labun. Reported by al-Tirmidhi and he considered it hasan, and al-Mustadrak, and Abu Dawud.
(2) In the letter of Abu Bakr to him: For twenty-four camels and below, from sheep for every five. When it reaches twenty-five to thirty-five, then a bint makhad (female). When it reaches thirty-six to forty-five, then a bint labun (female). When it reaches forty-six to sixty, then a hiqqah that is mountable by the camel. When it reaches sixty-one to seventy-five, then a jadha'ah. When it reaches seventy-six to ninety, then two bint labun. When it reaches ninety-one to one hundred twenty, then two hiqqahs mountable by the camel. When it exceeds one hundred twenty, then for every forty a bint labun, and for every fifty a hiqqah. And whoever has only four camels, there is no charity on them unless the owner wishes. When it reaches five camels, then a sheep. Reported by al-Bukhari and others.
Meaning: a sheep.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the obligatory zakah schedule for camels in the Hanafi school. The matn (text) presents the schedule in a concise form: for each nisab interval (5, 25, 36, 46, 61, 76, 91 up to 120), a specific animal must be given. The commentary then clarifies that this schedule applies to both types of camels (bukhti and arabi) unless evidence specifies otherwise, paralleling the general rulings for sheep and cattle.

The commentary defines the four key age categories:
- Bint makhad: a one-year-old she-camel (entering second year), named because its mother is pregnant with another.
- Bint labun: a two-year-old she-camel (entering third year), named because its mother is usually lactating for another calf.
- Hiqqah: a three-year-old she-camel (entering fourth year), named because it is fit to be ridden.
- Jadha'ah: a four-year-old she-camel (entering fifth year), named because it sheds its milk teeth.

The note from al-Ayni explains the wisdom behind requiring young females rather than old or male camels: young camels are not valid for sacrifice (thus preserving sacrificial animals) and it is easier on the owners; females are preferred due to their reproductive value.

The passage then reaches the matn phrase 'then for every five (camels), a sheep' (meaning from 5 to 24 camels, the obligation is a sheep for every 5). The commentary summarizes the entire schedule up to 120 camels, confirming it is agreed upon by the majority (jumhur) and proven by multiple authentic hadith narrated by a group of Companions.

Finally, a dispute (ikhtilaf) is noted regarding camels beyond 120. Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) held that the schedule does not restart; instead, for every 40 camels one gives a bint labun, and for every 50 a hiqqah. This view is supported by explicit texts in Sahih al-Bukhari and others. The text does not explicitly state the Hanafi position here, but the context implies that the Hanafi school holds that the schedule restarts from 5 after 120 (as indicated by the matn ending at 120). The passage provides two hadith proofs: the letter of Abu Bakr (as transmitted by Ibn 'Umar) and the similar letter of Abu Bakr recorded by al-Bukhari, both of which include the schedule up to 120 and then mention the 40/50 rule, supporting the Shafi'i view. The Hanafi position relies on a different interpretation or another hadith, but this is not elaborated in this passage.

Key Terms
استئناف (isti'naf): Restarting the schedule; in this context, the Hanafi view that the zakah schedule for camels begins anew from 5 after reaching 120.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
8 min
Zakat on Camels After 120: Isti'naf vs. Cycle
Dispute between Shafi'i and Hanafis on zakat schedule after 120 camels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on zakat for camels exceeding 120 and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using their respective proofs.

Core Idea
After 120 camels, Hanafis restart the schedule; Shafi'is continue the cycle.

Both schools agree on the zakat rates for camels from 1 to 120. The dispute arises after that threshold: does the schedule repeat from the beginning (isti'naf) or continue with a fixed pattern? This lesson dissects the arguments, proofs, and practical consequences of this inter-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed-upon rates up to 120
Identify the payment due at each nisab level from 5 to 120 camels, as recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari and other sources. All schools accept this.
These are: 5→1 sheep, 10→2, 15→3, 20→4, 25→bint makhad, 36→bint labun, 46→hiqqah, 61→jadha'ah, 76→2 bint labun, 91-120→2 hiqqahs.
2
Identify the point of disagreement after 120
Recognize that the dispute is not about the nisab up to 120, but about what happens when the herd exceeds 120. The Hanafi school says the schedule restarts (isti'naf); the Shafi'i school says a fixed cycle of 40 and 50 continues indefinitely.
The thamarah al-khilaf lies in the calculation of zakat for numbers above 120.
3
Present the Shafi'i position and its proof
State the Shafi'i view: after 120, for every 40 camels give a bint labun, for every 50 give a hiqqah. Their main proof is the hadith of Ibn 'Umar in Sahih al-Bukhari that explicitly states this schedule.
The Ibn 'Umar hadith is considered authentic and explicitly mentions the 40/50 cycle after 120.
4
Present the Hanafi position and its proofs
State the Hanafi view: after 120, nothing is due until the excess reaches 5, then give a sheep for each 5; when the excess reaches 25, the camel obligations restart (bint makhad etc.). Their proofs include the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud and the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm that for less than 25 camels, sheep are due for every 5.
The Hanafis argue that the Ibn 'Umar hadith is general and not specific to the restart; the more specific 'Amr ibn Hazm hadith and the practice of the Companions support isti'naf.
5
Analyze the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Work through examples: 130 camels, 135, 145, etc. Compare the number and type of animals due under each school. Note that the Hanafi position often results in fewer animals due at lower excesses but matches at milestones.
This step solidifies understanding of how the dispute affects real zakat calculations.
