كتاب الزكاة زكاة الأموال ثم في كل خمس شاة مع الحقتين. وجوب ما ذكر إلى مئة وعشرين من الشاة في كل خمس وبنت مخاض من خمس وعشرين، وبنت لبون من ست وثلاثين، وحقة من ست وأربعين، وجذعة من إحدى وستين وبنتي لبون في ست وأربعين، وحقتين من إحدى وتسعين إلى مئة وعشرين، متفق عليه بين الجمهور وثابت عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم برواية جمع من الصحابة مخرجة في صحيح البخاري وجامع الترمذي وسنن أبي داود والنسائي وغيرهم. واختلف فيما بعد المئة والعشرين: فذهب الشافعي وغيره إلى أنه لا استئناف هاهنا، بل يجب بعده في كل أربعين بنت لبون، وفي كل خمسين حقة، وبه ورد التصريح في صحيح البخاري وغيره. فعن ابن عمر: إن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كتاب وكتاب الصدقة فلم يخرجه إلى عماله حتى قبض فقرنه بسيفه فلما قبض عمل به أبو بكر حتى قبض وعمر حتى قبض وكان فيه في خمس من الإبل شاة وفي عشر شاتان وفي خمس عشر ثلاث شياه وفي عشرين أربع شياه وفي خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض إلى خمس وثلاثين فإذا زادت ففيها ابنة لبون إلى خمس وأربعين فإذا زادت ففيها حقة إلى ستين فإذا زادت فجذعة إلى خمس وسبعين فإذا زادت ففيها ابنتا لبون إلى تسعين فإذا زادت ففيها حقتان إلى عشرين ومئة فإذا زادت على عشرين ومئة ففي كل خمسين حقة وفي كل أربعين ابنة لبون. وأصحابنا قالوا بالاستئناف أخذا من قول ابن مسعود: فإذا بلغت مئة وعشرين استقبلت الفريضة ففي كل خمس شاة فإذا بلغت خمساً وعشرين ففرائض الإبل، أخرجه الطحاوي بسند فيه انقطاع، ومثله رواه ابن أبي شيبة عن علي. وأيدوه بأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كتب في كتاب الصدقات الذي كتبه لعمرو بن حزم: ما كان أقل من خمس وعشرين ففيه الغنم في كل خمس ذود شاة، أخرجه إسحاق بن راهويه وأبو داود في المراسيل وغيرهما. وحاصل مذهبنا أنه إذا زادت على مئة وعشرين لا يجب شيء سوى الحقتين ما لم تبلغ الزيادة خمساً، فإذا بلغت خمساً ففيه الشاة مع الحقتين، وفي العشر شاتان معهما، وفي خمس عشرة ثلاث شياه معهما، وفي العشرين أربع شياه معهما، وفي خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض إلى مئة وخمسين أي من أول النصاب، ففيه ثلاث حقاق. ثم في مئة وخمس وأربعين بنت مخاض وحقتان. ثم مئة وخمسين ثلاث حقاق، ثم تستأنف ففي كل خمس شاة. ثم في كل خمس وعشرين بنت مخاض. ثم في ست وثلاثين بنت لبون. ثم في مئة وست وتسعين أربع حقاق إلى مئتين.
The Book of Zakat: Zakat on Wealth. Then in every five (camels) one sheep with the two hiqqahs. The obligation of what was mentioned up to one hundred and twenty (camels): a sheep in every five, a bint makhad from twenty-five, a bint labun from thirty-six, a hiqqah from forty-six, a jadha'ah from sixty-one, two bint labun from seventy-six, and two hiqqahs from ninety-one to one hundred and twenty. This is agreed upon by the majority and established from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) through narrations by a group of Companions found in Sahih al-Bukhari, Jami' al-Tirmidhi, Sunan Abi Dawud, al-Nasa'i, and others. They differed regarding what is after one hundred and twenty: al-Shafi'i and others held that there is no restart here, but rather after it, for every forty a bint labun is due and for every fifty a hiqqah, and explicit mention of this is found in Sahih al-Bukhari and others. From Ibn 'Umar: 'Verily the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) wrote the book of charity, but did not send it out to his governors until he passed away; he kept it tied to his sword. When he passed away, Abu Bakr acted upon it until he died, and 'Umar until he died. In it: for five camels, one sheep; for ten, two sheep; for fifteen, three sheep; for twenty, four sheep; for twenty-five, a bint makhad up to thirty-five; when they exceed, then for up to forty-five a bint labun; when they exceed, then for up to sixty a hiqqah; when they exceed, then for up to seventy-five a jadha'ah; when they exceed, then for up to ninety two bint labun; when they exceed, then for up to one hundred and twenty two hiqqahs; when they exceed one hundred and twenty, then for every fifty a hiqqah and for every forty a bint labun.' Our companions (Hanafis) said: with restart (isti'naf), taking from the saying of Ibn Mas'ud: 'When it reaches one hundred and twenty, the obligation is restarted, so for every five a sheep, and when it reaches twenty-five, then the camel obligations (begin).' Recorded by al-Tahawi with a chain containing a break, and similar was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah from 'Ali. They supported it by (the fact) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) wrote in the book of charity which he wrote for 'Amr ibn Hazm: 'Whatever is less than twenty-five, then on it is sheep: for every five she-camels one sheep.' Recorded by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Abu Dawud in al-Marasil, and others. The summary of our madhhab is that when the number exceeds one hundred and twenty, nothing is due except the two hiqqahs until the increase reaches five. When it reaches five, then a sheep along with the two hiqqahs; for ten, two sheep with them; for fifteen, three sheep with them; for twenty, four sheep with them; for twenty-five, a bint makhad up to one hundred and fifty, i.e. from the beginning of the nisab, so in it there are three hiqqahs. Then in one hundred and forty-five: a bint makhad and two hiqqahs. Then one hundred and fifty: three hiqqahs. Then it is restarted: for every five a sheep. Then for every twenty-five a bint makhad. Then for thirty-six a bint labun. Then for one hundred and ninety-six: four hiqqahs up to two hundred.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah deals with the zakat obligations on camels, specifically the divergence after reaching 120 camels. The author first presents the agreed-upon rates from one to 120 camels, supported by hadiths from the Companions and recorded in the major hadith collections. These are: for 5 camels: 1 sheep; 10: 2 sheep; 15: 3 sheep; 20: 4 sheep; 25: a bint makhad (one-year-old she-camel); 36: a bint labun (two-year-old); 46: a hiqqah (three-year-old); 61: a jadha'ah (four-year-old); 76: two bint labun; 91 to 120: two hiqqahs. All schools agree on these. The dispute arises after 120. Imam al-Shafi'i and others maintain that there is no restart (isti'naf); rather, the cycle continues with a fixed pattern: for every 40 camels a bint labun is due, and for every 50 a hiqqah. They support this with the hadith from Ibn 'Umar which explicitly states: after 120, for every 50 a hiqqah and for every 40 a bint labun. The Hanafis, however, follow the position of Ibn Mas'ud and 'Ali that after 120, the obligation restarts. This means that nothing beyond the two hiqqahs is due until the excess reaches a new nisab of 5 camels, at which point sheep are given for each 5 until reaching 25 camels, where the camel nisab begins again. They support this with the hadith of 'Amr ibn Hazm stating that for less than 25 camels, sheep are due for every 5. The Hanafi ruling is detailed: after 120, only the two hiqqahs are due until the increase reaches 5. Then, for each additional 5, one sheep is added alongside the original two hiqqahs. This continues until the total reaches 145 (120+25) where a bint makhad plus two hiqqahs are due. At 150, three hiqqahs are due. Then the nisab restarts: for every 5 camels a sheep, and when reaching 25, the camel obligations begin again: for 25 a bint makhad, for 36 a bint labun, for 196 four hiqqahs up to 200. The key difference is that Shafi'is have a fixed perpetual cycle, while Hanafis restart the schedule from the beginning after the first 120.

Key Terms
الاستئناف (al-isti'naf): The restarting of the zakat schedule from the beginning after reaching a certain nisab (here 120 camels).
الحقة (al-hiqqah): A she-camel in her fourth year (entering the fifth). Zakat payable on camels at this age.
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A she-camel in her second year (entering the third). Due at certain nisab thresholds.
بنت لبون (bint labun): A she-camel in her third year (entering the fourth). Due at certain thresholds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what zakat is due on 125 camels?
A) Two hiqqahs and one sheep
B) One bint labun and one hiqqah
C) Two hiqqahs
D) One bint makhad and two hiqqahs

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that after 120 camels, the zakat schedule restarts (isti'naf) with sheep for each additional 5, whereas the Shafi'i school maintains a fixed cycle of 40 and 50.

Camel and Cattle Zakat: The Fara'id and the Isti'naf Principle
Detailed zakat schedule for camels and cattle with Hanafi proofs and isti'naf rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakat fara'id for camels from 5 to 200 and for cattle at 30, and apply the Hanafi principle of isti'naf to calculate zakat on camels.

Core Idea
After 120 camels, the zakat schedule restarts with sheep for every five, then resumes camel fara'id at 150.

This lesson unpacks the detailed zakat schedule for camels and cattle according to the Hanafi school. You will learn the specific animals due at each threshold, the unique Hanafi ruling of isti'naf (restarting) after 120 and 150 camels, and the brief ruling for cattle. Understanding these calculations is essential for correctly fulfilling the obligation of zakat on livestock.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial nisab and fara'id
Begin with the first nisab (5 camels) and list the fara'id (e.g., sheep, bint makhad, bint labun, hiqqah) up to 120 camels as per the text.
The schedule before 120 is standard and agreed upon.
2
Apply the restarting (isti'naf) at 120
When the herd exceeds 120, no zakat is due on the increase until it reaches 5. Then for every 5 increase, a sheep is added to the two hiqqahs already due for the first 120. Continue this until 150.
This is the first isti'naf.
3
Apply the second restarting at 150
At 150, three hiqqahs are due, and then the schedule restarts again as if from 5, but with camel fara'id resuming: for every 25 a bint makhad, etc., up to 200.
After 150, the pattern repeats every 50.
4
Understand the continuation beyond 200
After 200, the isti'naf pattern from 150 repeats: for every 50 a hiqqah, with an option between hiqqah or bint labun for different intervals. The text gives examples up to 300.
The pattern is cyclical.
5
Note the cattle ruling
For 30 cows or buffaloes, zakat is one tabi' (male two-year-old) or tabi'ah (female), with a choice. No isti'naf is mentioned for cattle.
Cattle zakat is simpler with a single threshold.

Then for one hundred and forty-five (camels) a bint makhad and two hiqqahs. Then for one hundred and fifty three hiqqahs, then it restarts so for every five a sheep. Then for every twenty-five a bint makhad. Then for thirty-six a bint labun. Then for one hundred and ninety-six four hiqqahs up to two hundred. And the summary of our madhhab is that when it exceeds one hundred and twenty, nothing is obligatory except the two hiqqahs as long as the increase does not reach five, so when it reaches five then a sheep with the two hiqqahs, and for ten two sheep with them, and for fifteen three sheep with them, and for twenty four sheep with them, and for twenty-five a bint makhad up to one hundred and fifty, i.e., from the beginning of the nisab, so there are three hiqqahs. 1] His saying: four... etc.; three of them are obligatory in one hundred and fifty, and the fourth is obligatory in the extra forty-six. Then it restarts always as in the fifty after one hundred and fifty. And for thirty cows or buffaloes, a tabi' or tabi'ah. Then it restarts always as in the fifty after one hundred and fifty. Know that two restartings have been mentioned: one after one hundred and twenty, and the other after one hundred and fifty. Then after two hundred it restarts a restart like that mentioned after one hundred and fifty, until a hiqqah becomes obligatory for every fifty. A hiqqah, and he is given a choice between paying five bint labun from every forty, a bint labun. As Qadi Khan mentioned. [1] His saying: as in the fifty... etc.; this is a precaution against the restarting after one hundred and twenty, for there is no obligation of bint labun nor obligation of four hiqqahs due to lack of its nisab; because when it increased over two hundred and twenty by twenty-five, the entire nisab becomes one hundred and forty-five, so it is the nisab of bint makhad with the two hiqqahs, and when it increased by five, becoming one hundred and fifty, three hiqqahs become obligatory. As in al-'Inayah. [2] His saying: for every fifty a hiqqah; i.e., for every forty-six up to fifty as he expressed in his Mukhtasar. He said in al-Bahr: 'When it exceeds two hundred by five sheep, then a sheep with four hiqqahs, or five bint labun, and for ten two sheep with them, and for fifteen three sheep with them, and for twenty four with them, and when it reaches two hundred and twenty-five, then a bint makhad with them up to thirty-six, then bint labun with them up to forty-six, and two hundred then five hiqqahs up to two hundred and fifty, then it restarts likewise in two hundred and ninety-six six hiqqahs up to three hundred, and so on.' (3) His saying: tabi' or tabi'ah; he is given a choice between the male and female, because femininity in it is not considered a virtue.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the zakat obligations on camels and cattle, with a focus on the Hanafi rulings regarding the restarting (isti'naf) of the zakat schedule after certain thresholds. The text begins by listing the specific fara'id for camels from 145 to 200: at 145 camels, a bint makhad (one-year-old) and two hiqqahs (three-year-old); at 150, three hiqqahs; then the schedule restarts, meaning that for every five camels beyond 150, a sheep is due until reaching 25, then the camel fara'id resume with bint makhad, bint labun, etc. The author then presents the Hanafi position, quoting Ibn Mas'ud and the Prophet's letter to 'Amr ibn Hazm as evidence for the restarting after 120 camels. The Hanafi school holds that when the herd exceeds 120, no zakat is due on the increase until it reaches five, at which point a sheep is added to the two hiqqahs already required. The text then explains the two types of restarting: one after 120 and one after 150. After 200, the restart follows the pattern after 150, requiring a hiqqah for every 50 camels, with an option between four hiqqahs from every 50 or five bint labun from every 40. The commentary from al-`Inayah and al-Bahr clarifies these calculations, noting that the restart after 120 differs because it does not involve bint labun or four hiqqahs due to the nisab not reaching that threshold. Finally, the passage addresses cattle: for 30 cows or buffaloes, one tabi' (male two-year-old) or tabi'ah (female) is due, with a choice between male and female, as femininity does not imply superiority. The reliance on al-`Inayah and al-Bahr shows that this ruling is from the established Hanafi transmissions.

Key Terms
استئناف (isti'naf): Restarting of the zakat schedule; after reaching a certain number (120 or 150 for camels), the calculation begins anew as if from the start.
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A female camel in its second year (having entered the first year after birth). It is a specific category of zakat on camels.
حقة (hiqqah): A female camel in its third year (having entered the fourth year). It is a category used in zakat on camels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) One bint labun and one hiqqah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi zakat schedule for camels restarts after 120 and 150, requiring sheep for the increase between 120-150 and then resuming camel fara'id.

The Evidence for Isti'naf and the Shafi'i Dispute
Hanafi proofs for isti'naf and the related madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi proofs for isti'naf and explain the Shafi'i counterargument and the practical difference between the two schools.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school cites Ibn Mas'ud and the Prophet's letter to 'Amr ibn Hazm to support the restarting after 120 camels.

While the previous lesson outlined the schedule, this lesson examines the textual evidence that the Hanafi school uses to justify the isti'naf principle and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view. Understanding the proof is crucial for appreciating the ikhtilaf and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on isti'naf
After 120 camels, the zakat restarts with sheep for every five increase. This is the core of the dispute.
2
Analyze the first dalil: Ibn Mas'ud's statement
Ibn Mas'ud said: 'When it reaches 120, the prescribed (zakat) is resumed; for every five a sheep.' Reported by al-Tahawi with a break in the chain.
The chain is munqati' but the Hanafi school accepts it because it is supported by other evidence.
3
Analyze the second dalil: Prophet's letter to 'Amr ibn Hazm
The Prophet wrote: 'What is less than 25, then sheep are due for every five camels.' Reported by Ishaq ibn Rahawayh and Abu Dawud in al-Marasil.
This is a mursal hadith, but Hanafi jurists consider it reliable due to the context.
4
Compare with Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school does not recognize the restarting; they apply a continuous schedule based on qiyas. Their evidence is a different interpretation of the same texts.
The practical difference appears in the calculation for herds between 120-150.

Then for one hundred and forty-five (camels) a bint makhad and two hiqqahs. Then for one hundred and fifty three hiqqahs, then it restarts so for every five a sheep. Then for every twenty-five a bint makhad. Then for thirty-six a bint labun. Then for one hundred and ninety-six four hiqqahs up to two hundred. [2] And our companions said: with restarting, taking from the statement of Ibn Mas'ud: 'When it reaches one hundred and twenty, the prescribed (zakat) is resumed, so for every five a sheep, and when it reaches twenty-five, then the fara'id of camels.' Reported by al-Tahawi with a chain containing a break, and similar was narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah from 'Ali. And the summary of our madhhab is that when it exceeds one hundred and twenty, nothing is obligatory except the two hiqqahs as long as the increase does not reach five, so when it reaches five then a sheep with the two hiqqahs, and for ten two sheep with them, and for fifteen three sheep with them, and for twenty four sheep with them, and for twenty-five a bint makhad up to one hundred and fifty, i.e., from the beginning of the nisab, so there are three hiqqahs. 1] His saying: four... etc.; three of them are obligatory in one hundred and fifty, and the fourth is obligatory in the extra forty-six. (2) His saying: up to two hundred; and he is given a choice between paying four hiqqahs from every fifty, a hiqqah, and paying five bint labun from every forty, a bint labun. As Qadi Khan mentioned. Then it restarts always as in the fifty after one hundred and fifty. And for thirty cows or buffaloes, a tabi' or tabi'ah. Then it restarts always as in the fifty after one hundred and fifty. Then after two hundred it restarts a restart like that mentioned after one hundred and fifty, until a hiqqah becomes obligatory for every fifty. A hiqqah, and he is given a choice between paying five bint labun from every forty, a bint labun. As Qadi Khan mentioned. [1] His saying: as in the fifty... etc.; this is a precaution against the restarting after one hundred and twenty, for there is no obligation of bint labun nor obligation of four hiqqahs due to lack of its nisab; because when it increased over two hundred and twenty by twenty-five, the entire nisab becomes one hundred and forty-five, so it is the nisab of bint makhad with the two hiqqahs, and when it increased by five, becoming one hundred and fifty, three hiqqahs become obligatory. As in al-'Inayah. [2] His saying: for every fifty a hiqqah; i.e., for every forty-six up to fifty as he expressed in his Mukhtasar. He said in al-Bahr: 'When it exceeds two hundred by five sheep, then a sheep with four hiqqahs, or five bint labun, and for ten two sheep with them, and for fifteen three sheep with them, and for twenty four with them, and when it reaches two hundred and twenty-five, then a bint makhad with them up to thirty-six, then bint labun with them up to forty-six, and two hundred then five hiqqahs up to two hundred and fifty, then it restarts likewise in two hundred and ninety-six six hiqqahs up to three hundred, and so on.' (3) His saying: tabi' or tabi'ah; he is given a choice between the male and female, because femininity in it is not considered a virtue.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the zakat obligations on camels and cattle, with a focus on the Hanafi rulings regarding the restarting (isti'naf) of the zakat schedule after certain thresholds. The text begins by listing the specific fara'id for camels from 145 to 200: at 145 camels, a bint makhad (one-year-old) and two hiqqahs (three-year-old); at 150, three hiqqahs; then the schedule restarts, meaning that for every five camels beyond 150, a sheep is due until reaching 25, then the camel fara'id resume with bint makhad, bint labun, etc. The author then presents the Hanafi position, quoting Ibn Mas'ud and the Prophet's letter to 'Amr ibn Hazm as evidence for the restarting after 120 camels. The Hanafi school holds that when the herd exceeds 120, no zakat is due on the increase until it reaches five, at which point a sheep is added to the two hiqqahs already required. The text then explains the two types of restarting: one after 120 and one after 150. After 200, the restart follows the pattern after 150, requiring a hiqqah for every 50 camels, with an option between four hiqqahs from every 50 or five bint labun from every 40. The commentary from al-`Inayah and al-Bahr clarifies these calculations, noting that the restart after 120 differs because it does not involve bint labun or four hiqqahs due to the nisab not reaching that threshold. Finally, the passage addresses cattle: for 30 cows or buffaloes, one tabi' (male two-year-old) or tabi'ah (female) is due, with a choice between male and female, as femininity does not imply superiority. The reliance on al-`Inayah and al-Bahr shows that this ruling is from the established Hanafi transmissions.

Key Terms
استئناف (isti'naf): Restarting of the zakat schedule; after reaching a certain number (120 or 150 for camels), the calculation begins anew as if from the start.
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A female camel in its second year (having entered the first year after birth). It is a specific category of zakat on camels.
حقة (hiqqah): A female camel in its third year (having entered the fourth year). It is a category used in zakat on camels.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A consensus of the Companions.
C) A Quranic verse on sadaqah.
D) An analogy (qiyas) with cattle.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi isti'naf principle is grounded in specific prophetic and companion reports, despite their chain weakness, and is distinct from the Shafi'i continuous schedule.

Zakat Nisab for Cows: Basic Obligations and Definitions
Commentary on zakat nisab for cows, definitions, and restart patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakat obligations for cows at the nisab thresholds of 30, 40, and 60, and define tabe' and musinn.

Core Idea
For 30 cows, one tabe'; for 40, one musinn; for 60, two tabe' — a clear pattern with restart at higher numbers.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the foundational rules of zakat on cows and buffaloes. It establishes the nisab thresholds and defines the animals due. Understanding these basics is essential before delving into the calculations for larger herds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nisab thresholds
List the numbers at which zakat obligations change: 30, 40, 60, and the restart patterns after 120, 150, and 200.
The restart pattern after 200 is like after 150.
2
State the obligation at each nisab
For 30 cows: one tabe' (one-year-old calf). For 40 cows: one musinn (two-year-old cow). For 60 cows: double of 30, i.e., two tabe'.
The choice between male and female is given for tabe'.
3
Define the terms tabe' and musinn
Tabe' is a calf that has completed one year and follows its mother; musinn is a cow that has completed two years and cut its teeth.
These definitions are from the Arabic linguistic roots.
4
Explain the restart patterns
After 120, a new pattern begins; after 150, another; after 200, the pattern resets like after 150, with a hiqqah due for every 50 cows (by analogy from camels).
The commentary clarifies that the restart after 120 does not require bint labun or four hiqqahs due to nisab calculation.

The Book of Zakat: Zakat on Wealth. Then it restarts perpetually, as in the fifty that comes after one hundred and fifty. And for thirty cows or buffaloes, a tabe' (one-year-old calf) or tabe'ah (female). Then it restarts perpetually, as in the fifty after one hundred and fifty. Know that two restarts have been mentioned: one after one hundred and twenty, and the other after one hundred and fifty. After two hundred, it restarts with a restart like that mentioned after one hundred and fifty, such that a hiqqah (three-year-old she-camel, here analogy for cows) is due for every fifty. For thirty cows or buffaloes, a tabe' or tabe'ah. (The text earlier said) hiqqah, and between that one should give five bint labun from every forty bint labun. Thus mentioned by Qadi Khan. [1] His statement: 'as in the fifty... etc.'; this is a precaution against the restart after one hundred and twenty, for in it there is no obligation of bint labun, nor obligation of four hiqqahs, due to lack of nisab; because when five and twenty are added to two hundred and twenty, the total nisab becomes one hundred and forty-five, so it is a nisab of bint makhad with two hiqqahs. When five more are added, making it one hundred and fifty, three hiqqahs become due. Thus in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'for every fifty a hiqqah'; i.e., for every forty-six up to fifty, as he expressed in his Mukhtasar. He said in al-Bahr: 'When sheep increase beyond two hundred by five, there is a sheep in them with four hiqqahs, or five bint labun; for ten, two sheep with them; for fifteen, three sheep with them; for twenty, four sheep with them. When it reaches two hundred and twenty-five, there is a bint makhad with them up to thirty-six; then a bint labun with them up to forty-six; two hundred, then there are five hiqqahs up to two hundred and fifty; then it restarts similarly at two hundred and ninety-six with six hiqqahs up to three hundred, and so on. [3] His statement: 'tabe' or tabe'ah'; there is a choice between male and female; because femininity in it is not considered a virtue. Then for every forty, a musinn (two-year-old) or musinnah. And for what increases, it is calculated up to sixty, and in it (sixty) is double what is in thirty. Then for every forty, a musinn or musinnah. At-tabe': is that which has completed one year, and at-tabe'ah is its female. Al-musinn: that which has completed two years, and al-musinnah is its female. And for what increases, it is calculated up to sixty, and in it is double what is in thirty. Thus in al-'Inayah. [1] His statement: 'musinn or musinnah'; thus it occurred in the hadith of Mu'adh, narrated by the people of the Sunan. [2] His statement: 'at-tabe'': on the pattern of fa'eel, named because it follows its mother and walks with her. [3] His statement: 'al-musinn': with dammah on the meem and kasrah on the seen, with shaddah on the noon; derived from 'asnan' (teeth), meaning the emergence of the tooth. [4] His statement: 'and for what increases'; i.e., when it increases beyond forty, the obligation on the increase is proportionally; so for one extra, one-quarter tenth of a musinnah; for two, half a tenth of a musinnah; for three, three-quarters tenth of a musinnah; for four, one tenth of a musinnah, and so on. This is in the narration of al-Asl from Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, because the 'afw (pardon) is established by text contrary to analogy, and there is no text here. This is the choice of the author, and the author of al-Mukhtar, al-Kanz, al-Mawahib, and others. The second: nothing is due on the increase until it reaches fifty, then there is a musinnah and a quarter of a musinnah, or one-third of a tabe'; this is the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, because the basis of this nisab is that between every two intervals there is a break, and in every interval there is an obligation. The third: nothing on the increase until it reaches sixty; this is the narration of Asad ibn 'Amr from Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him, and it is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). It is the choice of the author of al-Multaqa, Jami' al-Fiqh, and in al-Muhit: it is the mid-most opinion, and in al-Yanabi' and al-Isbijabi: and upon it is the fatwa as in Radd al-Muhtar. And it is the most moderate of the opinions.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a detailed commentary on the zakat rules for cows and buffaloes. It begins by explaining the restart (isti'naf) pattern after reaching certain nisab thresholds. The author clarifies that after 120 cows, a restart occurs with a different rule, and after 150, another restart. After 200, the restart is like after 150, meaning for every 50 cows from that point, a hiqqah (a three-year-old she-camel analogized for cows) is due. The text then addresses the rule for 30 cows: a tabe' (one-year-old calf) is due, with choice between male and female. The commentary cites Qadi Khan and al-'Inayah to explain why the restart after 120 does not require bint labun or four hiqqahs: because the addition of 25 cows to 220 makes 145, which is a nisab of bint makhad (a one-year-old she-camel) with two hiqqahs, and further additions change the obligation. The passage also defines tabe' as a calf that has completed one year and follows its mother, and musinn as a two-year-old cow that has developed teeth. The key legal discussion is on the calculation for cows between 40 and 60. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad), Abu Hanifa's view is that every excess cow beyond 40 up to 60 is calculated proportionally: 1 cow extra requires 1/40 of a musinnah (since 40 cows require one musinnah). This is because the rule of 'afw (pardon of small numbers) is textually based and cannot be extended by analogy. The second view, from Hasan ibn Ziyad, says no zakat on the increase until 50, then a musinnah plus a fraction is due. The third view, from Asad ibn 'Amr and also the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), is that nothing is due on the increase until the number reaches 60 (the next complete nisab). The author concludes that the relied-upon fatwa (al-mufta bihi) according to later authorities is the third view, which is the most moderate (a'dal) because it avoids fractions and follows the pattern of fixed nisabs. This intra-Hanafi dispute demonstrates the principle of takhrij (derivation of rulings from principles) and tarjih (preference) within the madhhab.

Key Terms
تبيع (tabe'): A one-year-old calf (male or female) that has completed one year and follows its mother.
مسن (musinn): A two-year-old cow (male or female) that has developed teeth, indicating it has completed two years.
استئناف (isti'naf): A new start in the calculation of zakat after reaching a certain nisab threshold, where the pattern of obligations resets.
حقة (hiqqah): A three-year-old she-camel; in this context, used analogically for the zakat on cows when the number reaches 200-250.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What zakat is due on 60 cows according to the basic rule?
A) One tabe'
B) One musinn
C) Two tabe'
D) Two musinn

Key Takeaway
Zakat on cows is wajib with specific animals due at nisabs of 30 (tabe'), 40 (musinn), and 60 (two tabe'), and restart patterns for higher numbers.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Zakat for Surplus Cows (40-60)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for cows between 40 and 60.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi views on zakat for cows between 40 and 60, and state which view is the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
On surplus cows between 40 and 60, Hanafi imams disagree: proportional, wait until 50, or wait until 60 — the mufta bihi is to wait until 60.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding cows numbering between 40 and 60. Three positions are reported from Abu Hanifa and his companions, each based on a different legal reasoning. Understanding this dispute highlights the principles of takhrij and tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed number range
The dispute concerns cows between 40 and 60. After 40 cows (which require one musinn), what is due for the surplus?
The passage explicitly mentions 'In what increases is calculated up to sixty'.
2
State the three positions
First (Abu Hanifa in al-Asl): proportional calculation – e.g., for 45 cows, 1 musinn + 1/8 musinn. Second (Hasan ibn Ziyad): nothing until 50, then a musinn and a quarter. Third (Asad ibn 'Amr & Sahibayn): nothing until 60.
Each view is a riwayah (narration) from Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning
Abu Hanifa: based on qiyas, since no text exempts surplus. Sahibayn: istihsan relying on the pattern that each complete nisab has a fixed obligation, and small numbers are pardoned.
The commentary refers to 'afw (pardon) as text-based, so it cannot be extended by qiyas.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Later authorities (al-Multaqa, al-Muhit, al-Yanabi', al-Isbijabi) favor the third view (nothing until 60) as the most moderate (a'dal).
Radd al-Muhtar confirms this is the relied-upon fatwa.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under the first view, fractional payments are required for odd numbers; under the third, no zakat until the next complete nisab at 60.
This affects ease of calculation and compliance.

The Book of Zakat: Zakat on Wealth. Then it restarts perpetually, as in the fifty that comes after one hundred and fifty. And for thirty cows or buffaloes, a tabe' (one-year-old calf) or tabe'ah (female). Then it restarts perpetually, as in the fifty after one hundred and fifty. Know that two restarts have been mentioned: one after one hundred and twenty, and the other after one hundred and fifty. After two hundred, it restarts with a restart like that mentioned after one hundred and fifty, such that a hiqqah (three-year-old she-camel, here analogy for cows) is due for every fifty. [1] His statement: 'as in the fifty... etc.'; this is a precaution against the restart after one hundred and twenty, for in it there is no obligation of bint labun, nor obligation of four hiqqahs, due to lack of nisab; because when five and twenty are added to two hundred and twenty, the total nisab becomes one hundred and forty-five, so it is a nisab of bint makhad with two hiqqahs. When five more are added, making it one hundred and fifty, three hiqqahs become due. Thus in al-'Inayah. [2] His statement: 'for every fifty a hiqqah'; i.e., for every forty-six up to fifty, as he expressed in his Mukhtasar. And for what increases, it is calculated up to sixty, and in it is double what is in thirty. Thus in al-'Inayah. [1] His statement: 'musinn or musinnah'; thus it occurred in the hadith of Mu'adh, narrated by the people of the Sunan. [2] His statement: 'at-tabe'': on the pattern of fa'eel, named because it follows its mother and walks with her. [3] His statement: 'al-musinn': with dammah on the meem and kasrah on the seen, with shaddah on the noon; derived from 'asnan' (teeth), meaning the emergence of the tooth. [4] His statement: 'and for what increases'; i.e., when it increases beyond forty, the obligation on the increase is proportionally; so for one extra, one-quarter tenth of a musinnah; for two, half a tenth of a musinnah; for three, three-quarters tenth of a musinnah; for four, one tenth of a musinnah, and so on. This is the choice of the author, and the author of al-Mukhtar, al-Kanz, al-Mawahib, and others. And it is the most moderate of the opinions.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a detailed commentary on the zakat rules for cows and buffaloes. It begins by explaining the restart (isti'naf) pattern after reaching certain nisab thresholds. The author clarifies that after 120 cows, a restart occurs with a different rule, and after 150, another restart. After 200, the restart is like after 150, meaning for every 50 cows from that point, a hiqqah (a three-year-old she-camel analogized for cows) is due. The text then addresses the rule for 30 cows: a tabe' (one-year-old calf) is due, with choice between male and female. The commentary cites Qadi Khan and al-'Inayah to explain why the restart after 120 does not require bint labun or four hiqqahs: because the addition of 25 cows to 220 makes 145, which is a nisab of bint makhad (a one-year-old she-camel) with two hiqqahs, and further additions change the obligation. The passage also defines tabe' as a calf that has completed one year and follows its mother, and musinn as a two-year-old cow that has developed teeth. The key legal discussion is on the calculation for cows between 40 and 60. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad), Abu Hanifa's view is that every excess cow beyond 40 up to 60 is calculated proportionally: 1 cow extra requires 1/40 of a musinnah (since 40 cows require one musinnah). This is because the rule of 'afw (pardon of small numbers) is textually based and cannot be extended by analogy. The second view, from Hasan ibn Ziyad, says no zakat on the increase until 50, then a musinnah plus a fraction is due. The third view, from Asad ibn 'Amr and also the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), is that nothing is due on the increase until the number reaches 60 (the next complete nisab). The author concludes that the relied-upon fatwa (al-mufta bihi) according to later authorities is the third view, which is the most moderate (a'dal) because it avoids fractions and follows the pattern of fixed nisabs. This intra-Hanafi dispute demonstrates the principle of takhrij (derivation of rulings from principles) and tarjih (preference) within the madhhab.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Zakat on the Increase: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute (40–60 Cows)
Expands on zakat nisab for cows and the intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nisab thresholds for cows in the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding zakat on the increase between 40 and 60, identifying the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
When cows exceed 40, some say proportional zakat; the relied-upon view says nothing until 60.

Zakat on cattle is wajib when the nisab is reached and one lunar year passes. For cows, the first nisab is 30 (one tabi') and 40 (one musinn). But what about the gap between 40 and 60? Abu Hanifa reportedly gave two views, and the Sahibayn held a third. This lesson unpacks that dispute and shows which opinion is operative for fatwa today.

Guided Path
1
Establish the Base Nisab for Cows
Identify the minimum number of cows that trigger zakat (30: one tabi'; 40: one musinn) and note the terminology: tabi' (1-year-old) and musinn (2-year-old).
The passage also defines these terms and traces them to hadith.
2
Identify the Disputed Zone (40–60)
When cows exceed 40 but are less than 60, two positions emerge: proportional zakat on the excess (Abu Hanifa, first narration) vs. no zakat until the next full nisab of 60 (Sahibayn and another narration from Abu Hanifa).
The text labels these as 'qawl' and 'qawluhuma'.
3
Analyze the Reasoning for Each Position
The proportional view argues that exemption between nisabs is text-based and cannot be extended by qiyas, so the default is proportional calculation. The other view cites the hadith of Mu'adh that nothing is taken 'between that' (30 and 40) and applies the same principle to the 40–60 gap.
The dalil is a hadith, though its application is by qiyas (analogy).
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi
Later authoritative works (al-Bahr, al-Durr al-Mukhtar) adopt the Sahibayn view, making it the operative opinion for fatwa.
Authority_weight shifts from zahir al-riwayah to later tarjih.
5
Apply to a Practical Case
Given 55 cows, apply the mufta bihi: only one musinnah is due for the first 40; the extra 15 are exempt until 60. If a student incorrectly applies proportional zakat, they would add a fraction of a musinnah.
This demonstrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

Then for every forty [cows], a musinn or musinnah. And for what exceeds [forty], it is calculated until sixty, and in it [sixty] is double what is in thirty. Then for every forty, a musinn or musinnah. (2) At-tabi': that which has completed one year, and at-tabi'ah is its female. And al-musinn: that which has completed two years, and al-musinnah is its female. And for what exceeds, it is calculated until sixty, and in it is double what is in thirty. Thus in al-'Inayah. His statement: al-musinn; with damma on the mim, kasr on the sin, and tashdid on the nun; derived from teeth, meaning the emergence of the tooth. His statement: and for what exceeds; that is, if it exceeds forty, zakat is due on the excess proportionately; so for one extra, a quarter-tenth of a musinnah; for two, half-tenth of a musinnah; for three, three-quarters tenth of a musinnah; and so on; because exemption (i.e., non-obligation) between the nisabs is established by text, contrary to qiyas, and there is no text here. Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an expansion on the rules of zakat for cattle (specifically cows/bulls) and sheep/goats. It begins by stating the nisab for cows: for every forty cows, one musinn (a two-year-old) is due. For what exceeds forty, there are multiple views. The author first presents the view attributed to Abu Hanifa that the increase is calculated proportionally: for one extra cow, one quarter-tenth of a musinnah; for two, half-tenth, etc., until sixty, at which point two musinnahs are due (double of thirty). This view is based on the principle that exemption (non-obligation) between nisabs is established by text (nas) and cannot be extended by analogy. However, since there is no nas for the intermediate amounts, the default is that zakat applies proportionally. Then the author notes another narration from Abu Hanifa—and it is the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani)—that nothing is due on the excess until it reaches sixty. This is the view upon which fatwa is given, as stated in authoritative later works like al-Nahr, al-Bahr, and al-Durr al-Mukhtar. The passage supports this with the hadith of Mu'adh where the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded not to take anything between the nisabs (meaning between thirty and forty for sheep). The passage then continues to list the nisab for cows beyond sixty: at seventy, one tabi' (one-year-old) and one musinnah; at eighty, two musinnahs; at ninety, three tabi's; at one hundred, two tabi's and one musinnah; at one hundred ten, one tabi' and two musinnahs; at one hundred twenty, four tabi's or three musinnahs; and so on. Finally, the passage covers zakat on sheep and goats: for every forty, one shat is due, with the condition that it be a thaniyy (one that has completed one year), not a jadha' (younger).

Key Terms
مُسِنّ (musinn): A cow that has completed two years (entered its third year).
تَبِيع (tabi'): A cow that has completed one year (entered its second year).
شَاة (shah): A sheep or goat (one head) that meets the age condition (thaniyy, at least one year old).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what zakat is due on 55 cows?
A) One musinnah and a quarter-tenth of a musinnah
B) One musinnah only
C) Nothing, because 55 is between 40 and 60
D) Two musinnahs

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position for zakat on cows is that no zakat is due on the increase between 40 and 60 cows; only the first 40 trigger one musinnah.

Zakat on Cattle: Nisab Thresholds and the Exemption Between Nisab
Ruling on zakat nisab for cows and sheep with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakat nisab thresholds for cows and sheep/goats, explain the principle of 'afw (exemption) between nisabs, and identify the relied-upon Hanafi position in the intra-madhhab dispute on the amount exempt.

Core Idea
No zakat is due on the surplus between two nisab thresholds — this exemption is established by explicit hadith, not analogy.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya lays out the precise nisab thresholds for zakat on cattle (cows and sheep/goats). It then introduces a crucial Hanafi principle: the 'afw (exemption) that no zakat is due on the amount between one nisab and the next. This principle is grounded in a hadith of Mu'adh and overrides the normal analogical reasoning (qiyas) that would suggest zakat on every animal. Understanding the 'afw is essential for correct calculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic thresholds for cows and sheep
The matn states: every 30 cows require a tabi' (1-year-old calf), every 40 require a musinnah (2-year-old cow); every 40 sheep/goats require one sheep.
2
Trace the expansion of nisabs for cows
The commentary shows the pattern up to 120 cows: 60 → two tabi's; 70 → tabi' + musinnah; 80 → two musinnahs; 90 → three tabi's; 100 → two tabi's + musinnah; 110 → tabi' + two musinnahs; 120 → four tabi's or three musinnahs. This pattern continues indefinitely.
The pattern alternates based on multiples of 30 and 40.
3
Locate the principle of 'afw
The commentary states that no zakat is due on the amount between two nisab thresholds (e.g., between 30 and 40, between 40 and 60, etc.). This is called 'afw and is established by explicit text (nas).
The 'afw applies to the surplus, not to the entire lower nisab.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa (one narration) held that no zakat is due on the increase until it reaches 60; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two companions) said no zakat on excess at all. The fatwa is on the latter view, meaning even a small surplus above a nisab is exempt until the next complete nisab is reached.
The ikhtilaf is only on the treatment of the surplus; all agree on the basic thresholds.
5
Evaluate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Mu'adh: 'The Messenger of Allah commanded me not to take anything in between that' (i.e., between thirty and forty). This explicit command (nas) overrides qiyas, establishing the 'afw as a dispensation (rukhsah).
The hadith is ahad but its meaning is clear (dalala qati').

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the nisab (thresholds) for zakat on cattle (cows and sheep/goats). The matn states the basic rule: for cows, every 30 require a tabi' (one-year-old calf) and every 40 require a musinnah (two-year-old cow); for sheep/goats, every 40 require one sheep. The commentary also gives the detailed thresholds for sheep up to 400, then every 100. The passage emphasizes that the exemption is based on explicit text (nas) not analogy.

Key Terms

مُسِنَّة (musinnah): A two-year-old cow (entering its third year).
العفو (al-afw): The exemption from zakat on the amount between two nisab thresholds.

Zakat Exemption for Working Animals: Donkeys, Mules, and Non-Grazing Livestock
Ruling with proof on zakat exemption for non-trade working animals, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for donkeys, mules, working animals, and stall-fed animals, and identify the condition that triggers zakat obligation on them.

Core Idea
No zakat on working, pregnant, or stall-fed animals unless kept for trade.

Many people assume that all livestock are subject to zakat, but the Hanafi school distinguishes between grazing livestock (sa'imah) and non-grazing animals used for work or breeding. This lesson examines the ruling on donkeys, mules, working animals, pregnant animals, and stall-fed animals, showing that the default is no zakat—unless the animals are held as trade goods. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi difference on this issue and the hadith evidence that supports the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Animals and Default Ruling
Recognize the categories mentioned: donkeys, mules, working animals (عوامل), pregnant animals (حوامل), and stall-fed animals (علوفة). The matn states there is no zakat on them.
The condition 'not for trade' is key—if they are for trade, zakat applies.
2
Locate the Dalil (Hadith)
The commentator cites the hadith: 'There is no sadaqah on working animals' (ليس في العوامل صدقة). Note its source (Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni) and its authority level (ahad hadith).
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but qat'i al-dalala in negating zakat for working animals.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
The passage notes that the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) had a view that zakat is permissible (or due) on these animals, but the zahir al-riwaya (relied-upon transmission) is no zakat, as most books follow.
Ibn al-Humam mentions that the evidence (dalil) actually favors the permissibility view, but the madhhab relies on the well-known transmission.
4
Apply the Condition: For Trade
The ruling changes if the animals are kept with the intention of trade (نية التجارة). In that case, they are treated like other trade goods (عروض التجارة) and zakat is wajib if the nisab is met.
This condition is crucial—many people mistakenly think all animals are exempt, but trade animals are not.
5
General Principle: Exemption of Non-Sa'imah
The general rule is that zakat on livestock is restricted to grazing animals (sa'imah). Non-grazing animals (like stall-fed, working) are excluded unless for trade. This is based on the hadith and the broad principle that zakat is not due on animals that are not free-grazing.
Stall-fed animals (علوفة) are explicitly mentioned as excluded from the general obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is nothing (zakat) on a mule and donkey that are not for trade, nor on working animals, pregnant animals, or stall-fed animals. [Commentary: That is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), and it is what most (books) have transmitted, and from him (Abu Hanifa) there is a permissibility of zakat? Footnote: 'not for trade' is a qualification; because if they are (kept) with the intention of trade, zakat is due as in other trade goods. Footnote: 'nor on working animals' due to the hadith: 'There is no sadaqah on working animals' recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani, al-Daraqutni, etc. And in its meaning are pregnant animals (hawaamil), and the reports of zakat obligation are restricted by the condition of 'sa'imah' (grazing), so stall-fed animals are excluded.]
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding zakat on donkeys, mules, and working animals. The matn states that no zakat is due on these animals if they are not for trade. The commentary clarifies that the default ruling (zahir al-riwaya) is no zakat, but there is a narration from Abu Hanifa (perhaps) that allows zakat on them, and that view is of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The evidence (dalil) is the hadith 'There is no sadaqah on working animals.' The condition 'not for trade' is crucial: if intended for trade, zakat applies like other merchandise. The animals are divided into three categories: working animals (عوامل), pregnant animals (حوامل), and stall-fed (علوفة). The general evidence restricts zakat to sa'imah (grazing livestock), thus excluding non-grazing animals. The passage shows an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the zahir al-riwaya (no zakat) versus the permissibility view of the two companions.

Key Terms
عوامل (awaamil): Working animals used for plowing, carrying, etc.
علوفة (aluufah): Stall-fed animals (not grazing freely).
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmission from the early Hanafi masters, considered the primary position.

Key Takeaway
Donkeys, mules, working animals, pregnant animals, and stall-fed animals are not subject to zakat unless they are held for trade.

Zakat on Pastured Sheep and Exemptions
Ruling on zakat nisab for sheep and exemptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakat nisab for pastured sheep and identify which animals are exempt from zakat according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Zakat on livestock is only due on pastured (sa'imah) animals; working and fed animals are exempt.

The Hanafi school specifies clear thresholds for zakat on sheep that graze freely. This lesson covers the exact nisab increments and the exemptions for certain animals, including draft animals and young stock. Understanding these rules prevents common errors in calculating zakat on livestock.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is paying zakat on pastured sheep. The ruling is that it is wajib (obligatory) based on ahad hadith.
In Hanafi usul, wajib is established by zanni evidence, unlike fard which is qat'i.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith of Anas (ra) which specifies the rates for sa'imah sheep. The hadith is cited in the commentary (e.g., Sahih al-Bukhari).
The absence of mention of other animals in the hadith is taken as evidence for their exemption.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith explicitly states numbers and rates for pastured sheep. By implication, animals that are not pastured or are not sheep/goats are not included. The Hanafi school limits the obligation to sa'imah.
This is an example of dialectical implication (mafhum al-mukhalafah) in Hanafi usul.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding zakat on horses: Abu Hanifa exempts male horses, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold zakat is due. The mufta bihi opinion is no zakat on horses unless for trade.
The ikhtilaf is based on different interpretations of the hadith's scope.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Al-Marghinani) prefer the opinion of non-obligation for horses, aligning with zahir al-riwayah. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Tarjih is based on strength of evidence and established practice.

Commentary
The passage states the zakat increments for pastured sheep (sa'imah). The text clarifies that zakat is not due on mules and donkeys unless they are kept for trade. Similarly, draft animals (used for plowing), pregnant animals, and animals fed with fodder rather than grazing are exempt, as the obligation is tied to sa'imah (grazing). Young animals (lamb, foal, calf) are only counted with adults to complete the nisab, not separately. Male horses are exempt from zakat in the relied-upon view, with a conflicting report about females. These rulings are based on hadith evidence cited in the commentary.

Key Terms
سائمة (sa'imah): Livestock that graze freely for most of the year without being fed fodder.
عوامل ('awamil): Draft animals used for work like plowing.

Zakat on sheep is wajib only when they are pastured (sa'imah) and reach the nisab thresholds; working, fed, and young animals are exempt or only counted as followers.

Zakat on Young Livestock: Followers of the Herd
Ruling with proof on zakat for young livestock

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for young livestock (hammal, fasil, ajil) and apply the principle that they are only zakatable as followers of mature animals.

Core Idea
No zakat is due on young livestock alone; they only count toward nisab as followers of mature animals.

In Hanafi zakat law, not all livestock are treated equally. Young animals—such as lambs (hammal), camel calves (fasil), and cow calves (ajil)—are not individually subject to zakat. They only contribute to the nisab (minimum threshold) when they accompany mature animals. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its scriptural basis, and the practical implications for farmers and herders.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the act (zakat on young livestock) and the ruling: no zakat is due on hammal, fasil, or ajil when they are alone. They only count as part of a full nisab when accompanying mature animals.
The ruling applies to all three categories of young animals mentioned.
2
Locate the Dalil
The text cites the revealed sources: 'the Shari'ah came regarding large animals, not regarding small animals alone.' The nisab amounts are tawqifi (fixed by revelation) and not subject to qiyas.
The absence of explicit obligation on young animals is itself the dalil.
3
Understand the 'Follower' Principle
Young animals become zakatable only as 'followers' (taba) of mature animals. Example: 39 lambs + 1 mature sheep = zakat due on 40 because the young follow the mature.
This applies similarly to camels and cows.
4
Apply the Exception
If all mature animals die, leaving only young ones, no zakat is due on the young alone. This is Abu Hanifa's last opinion, based on the principle that fixed measures are not analogized.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العواملُ : التي أُعِدَتْ للعمل، كإثارة الأرض. والحوامل : التي أُعِدَتْ لحمل الأثقال. والعلوفة : التي تُعطى العلف ، وهي ضد السائمة.
(ولا في حمل"، وفصيل، وعجل إلا تبعاً للكبير
[٤] لذا
[٢] قوله : ولا في حمل ؛ الحَمَل بفتحتين : ولد الشَّاةِ في السنة الأولى. والفصيلُ ككريم : ولد الناقة قبل أن يصير ابن مخاض. والعجيل : ولد البقر حين تضعُهُ أمّه إلى شهر. كذا في «المغرب»(۳).
[٣] قوله : إلا تبعاً للكبير؛ وإن كان واحداً ، كما إذا كان مع تسع وثلاثين حملاً مسن يجب ما يجب في الأربعين، وكذا في الإبل والبقر، ولو ماتت الكبار كلّها ولم يبقَ إلا الصغار لا يجب فيها شيء عنده وهو آخر أقواله ؛ لأنّ المقادير لا تدخلها القياس ، وقد ورد الشرع في الكبار، لا في الصغار منفردة.
Working animals: those prepared for work, like tilling the earth. Pack animals: those prepared for carrying loads. Stabled animals: those given fodder, which is the opposite of pasturing (sa'imah).
(And there is no [zakat] on a hammal, nor on a fasil, nor on an ajil, except as a follower of the large [animals].
[4] Therefore
[2] His saying: "nor on a hammal" — hammal (with two fatha) is the offspring of a sheep in its first year. Fasil (like kareem) is the offspring of a camel before it becomes a ibn makhad. Ajil is the offspring of a cow from when its mother gives birth up to a month. As in 'al-Mughrib' (3).
[3] His saying: "except as a follower of the large" — even if it is one, as if along with thirty-nine hammal there is a mature [sheep], what is obligatory for forty is obligatory. Similarly for camels and cows. If all the large animals die and only the small remain, no zakat is due on them according to him (Abu Hanifa), and this is his last opinion, because the measures [of nisab] are not subject to analogy, and the Shari'ah came regarding large animals, not regarding small animals alone.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, explaining the ruling on zakat for young livestock. The author first defines three categories of animals: working animals (used for labor), pack animals (used for transport), and stabled animals (fed fodder). These are contrasted with pasturing animals (sa'imah) on which zakat is due. The main ruling states that young animals—hammal (sheep lamb in first year), fasil (camel calf before weaning), and ajil (cow calf up to one month)—are not individually zakatable. They only count toward zakat as followers of the larger herd. For example, if someone has 39 lambs and one mature sheep, zakat is due on the 40 lambs as if they were 40 mature sheep because the young follow the mature. However, if all mature animals die and only young remain, no zakat is due on the young alone. The reasoning is that the nisab amounts are determined by revelation (tawqif), not analogy. The revealed texts mention large animals as the basis for zakat, not young ones by themselves.

Key Terms
حمل (hammal): The offspring of a sheep in its first year.
فصيل (fasil): The offspring of a camel before it becomes an ibn makhad (i.e., before one year).
عجيل (ajil): The offspring of a cow from birth up to one month.
تابع (tabi): Follower; the principle that young animals count as followers of mature animals for zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat on 5 camels (nisab)
B) No zakat
C) Zakat on 10 camels
D) Zakat on 1 camel

Key Takeaway
Young livestock are not zakatable individually; they only count as part of the nisab when accompanying mature animals.

Zakat on Horses: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on zakat for horses and explain the tarjih (preferred ruling) between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa held zakat on mixed horse herds; the Sahibayn said no zakat at all — the fatwa follows the Sahibayn.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: whether zakat is due on horses. Abu Hanifa, relying on a hadith and qiyas, ruled that mixed herds require payment, while his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, held that no zakat applies. The passage also details sub-disputes on male and female horses alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is zakat on horses. The core dispute is between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) on whether any zakat is due.
Both agree that horses for trade are zakatable; dispute is on non-trade horses.
2
List the scenarios and Abu Hanifa's positions
Three scenarios: male horses alone, female horses alone, mixed herds. For males alone: no zakat (preponderant). For females alone: one narration says no, another says yes (latter preponderant due to breeding possibility). For mixed herds: one dinar per horse or one-quarter of a tenth of value.
The 'borrowed stallion' argument explains the preponderance on females.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold no zakat on horses at all, regardless of gender or mixing.
They base their view on stronger hadith.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa uses the hadith 'On every horse one dinar' (weak) and qiyas on pasturing animals. The Sahibayn use the hadith 'No sadaqah on a Muslim for his slave or his horse' (stronger chain).
The Hanafi response is that Abu Hanifa's qiyas is overridden by the stronger text.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later scholars (al-Hawi, al-Yanabi', etc.) give fatwa on the Sahibayn's view: no zakat on horses. This is the operative ruling.
Ibn al-Humam preferred Abu Hanifa's view, but the majority fatwa is otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في ذكور الخيل منفردة، وكذا في إناثها في رواية
[4]قوله : ولا في ذكور ؛ أي إن كانت ذكور الخيل منفردةً ليس معها أُنثى لا يجبُ فيها شيء على الراجح ، وروي الوجوب، وإن كانت الإناث منفردة فكذلك في رواية ؛ لعدم تحقق النماء فيها بالتوالد كما في الذكور منفردة، وفي رواية الوجوب، وهـو الراجح ; لأنها تتناسلُ بالفحل المستعار،
وإن كان الخيل مختلطاً بعضه ذكور وبعضه إنات يجب في كلِّ فرس دينار، وإن شاءَ قومه وأعطى ربع عشر قيمته.
وهذا التخيير مروي عن إبراهيمَ النَّخْعِي ، أخرجه محمد له في كتاب «الآثار»، وفي «سننِ الدَّارَقُطني بسند ضعيف جداً مرفوعاً: «في كل فرس دينار»، هذا كله عنده (أي عند أبي حنيفة). وعندهما لا زكاة في الخيل مطلقاً؛ لحديث: «ليس على المسلم في عبده وفرسه صدقة»، أخرجته السنّة، في رواية : عفوت عن صدقة الخيل والرقيق»، أخرجه الترمذي وأبو داود وغيرهما، ورجح الحاوي قولهما، وفي «الينابيع» و«الجواهر» و«الخانية»(۳) و«الكافي» و«البزازيّة ) : إنَّ عليه الفتوى ، ورجح ابن الهمام (قوله).
And no [zakat] on male horses alone, and similarly on female horses according to one narration.
[4] His saying: 'nor on male horses' means: if male horses are alone without a female, nothing is due on them according to the preponderant view, and obligation has been narrated. And if females are alone, similarly in one narration, due to the lack of actual growth by breeding as in male horses alone. In another narration obligation, and that is preponderant because they breed through a borrowed stallion.
And if the horses are mixed, some males and some females, one dinar is due on every horse, or if he wishes he may assess their value and pay one quarter of a tenth of their value.
This option is narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i; Muhammad reported it for him in the book 'al-Athar'. And in Sunan al-Daraqutni with a very weak chain, marfu': 'On every horse one dinar'. All this according to him (Abu Hanifa). And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), there is no zakat on horses at all, due to the hadith: 'There is no sadaqah on a Muslim for his slave or his horse', which the Sunan have transmitted; in one narration: 'I have forgiven the sadaqah of horses and slaves.' Al-Hawi preferred their view, and in al-Yanabi', al-Jawahir, al-Khaniyya, al-Kafi, and al-Bazzaziyya: the fatwa is upon it, and Ibn al-Humam preferred his (Abu Hanifa's) view.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage discusses the zakat on horses within the Hanafi school. It begins by stating that there is no zakat on male horses alone, and likewise on female horses alone according to one narration. The commentator explains that this is because male horses alone do not produce growth (nama') through breeding, and females alone also lack growth without a male. However, a rival narration imposes zakat because females can breed through a borrowed stallion, and this narration is considered preponderant by some. For mixed herds (both males and females), the ruling is clear: one dinar per horse, or alternatively, the owner may calculate one-quarter of a tenth of the value. This option is attributed to Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and is part of Abu Hanifa's position. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the opposite: no zakat on horses at all, based on the hadith that there is no sadaqah on a Muslim's slave or horse. They rely on the version 'I have forgiven the sadaqah of horses and slaves.' The commentator notes that many later scholars (al-Hawi, al-Yanabi', al-Jawahir, etc.) give fatwa according to the two companions' view, though Ibn al-Humam preferred Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
خيل (khayl): Horses, including both males and females.
دينار (dinar): A gold coin; the zakat amount per horse in mixed herds.
قيمة (qimat): Monetary value; in zakat, one may pay the value instead of the specified item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Zakat is due on every horse, regardless of gender or mixing.
B) Zakat is due only on mixed herds (males and females together).
C) No zakat is due on horses at all.
D) Zakat is due on female horses only.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for horses is resolved in favor of no zakat, with the Sahibayn's view being the operative fatwa.

Zakat on Mixed Horses: Ruling and Option to Pay Value
Ruling on zakat for mixed horse herds with option to pay value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for mixed grazing horses and explain the option to pay the value in zakat and other obligations.

Core Idea
For mixed grazing horses, zakat is due — either one dinar per horse or quarter-tenth of its value, with the option to pay value.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a specific case: zakat on horses that are both male and female and grazing (sa'imah). The ruling gives two options for payment: a fixed amount per head or a percentage of the value. The text also clarifies the permissibility of paying monetary value in zakat and similar obligations, a point often misunderstood. We will dissect the ruling, its proof, and the internal Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and conditions
Determine what type of horses this ruling covers (mixed males and females, sa'imah) and the conditions for zakat (value reaching nisab).
The ruling applies only to mixed horses that graze freely, not stalled ones.
2
Locate the primary ruling and alternative
State the two options: one dinar per horse or a quarter-tenth of the market value. Understand that these are alternatives, not cumulative.
The text says 'or' (أو), indicating choice.
3
Analyze the proof and its weakness
Examine the hadith cited from Sunan al-Daraqutni and its chain weakness. Understand why it does not establish certainty (qat').
The weak chain means the hadith is zanni al-thubut, not sufficient alone for obligation.
4
Understand the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf
Contrast the preponderant view (wajib) with the narration that says it is not wajib. Identify the basis: potential reproduction through a borrowed stallion.
The tarjih is based on the likelihood of growth, not certainty.
5
Apply the permissibility of paying value
Recognize that paying the monetary value instead of the animal is allowed in zakat, kaffarah, ushr, and nadhr. Understand the rationale: the purpose is delivering benefit to the poor.
This is a general principle in the Hanafi school, but subject to conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كل فرس من المختلط به الذكور والإناث سائمة دينار، أو ربع عشر قيمتِهِ نصاباً. وجاز دفعُ القيم فى الزَّكاة، والكفارة، والعشر، والنذر. لعدم تحقق النماء فيها بالتوالد كما في الذكور منفردة، وفي رواية الوجوب، وهـو الراجح ؛ لأنها تتناسلُ بالفحل المستعار، وإن كان الخيل مختلطاً بعضه ذكور وبعضه إنات يجب في كلِّ فرس دينار، وإن شاءَ قومه وأعطى ربع عشر قيمته. وهذا التخيير مروي عن إبراهيمَ النَّخْعِي ، أخرجه محمد له في كتاب «الآثار»، وفي «سننِ الدَّارَقُطني بسند ضعيف جداً مرفوعاً: «في كل فرس دينار»، هذا كله .

[٢) قوله : وجاز...الخ ؛ يعني يجوز دفع القيمة في الزكاة بأن لا يدفع الشاةَ الواجبة بل قيمتها، وكذا في كفارات مالية، وفي النذر بأن نذر أن يتصدَّقَ شاةً مثلاً، وفي عُشرِ الأرض يدفعُ عشرَ ما خرج أو قيمته ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الأمر بأداء الزَّكاة إلى الفقير؛ لأجل إيصال الرِّزْقِ إلى الفقراء، ويستوي فيه العين وقيمته، ولم يوجد دليل يمنع أداء القيمة.
And for every horse among those mixed with males and females that are grazing, one dinar, or a quarter-tenth of its value, [provided it reaches] the nisab. And paying the value is permissible in zakat, and expiation, and ushr, and vow. Because growth through reproduction is not realized in them, as in males alone, and in one narration [it is] wajib, and it is the preponderant view, because they reproduce by a borrowed stallion. If the horses are mixed, some males and some females, there is due for every horse one dinar, and if he wishes he may value them and give a quarter-tenth of their value. This option is narrated from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, transmitted by Muhammad for him in the book al-Athar, and in Sunan al-Daraqutni with a very weak chain as marfu': 'For every horse, one dinar.' This is all.
[1] His saying: 'nisaban' – a circumstantial accusative of 'value', meaning state that it has reached the amount of nisab.
[2] His saying: 'and it is permissible...' meaning that paying the value is permissible in zakat, i.e., he does not pay the obligatory sheep but its value, and similarly in financial expiations, and in a vow that he vowed to give in charity a sheep for example, and in the ushr of land he pays a tenth of what comes out or its value; because the command to pay zakat to the poor is for the sake of delivering sustenance to the poor, and the actual item and its value are equal in that, and there is no evidence found that prevents paying the value.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam Muhammad's al-Athar and commentary on it. It addresses zakat on horses that are mixed (both male and female) and grazing (sa'imah). The ruling: for each horse, one dinar is due, or alternatively, a quarter-tenth of its value if the owner prefers to assess its market value. This option applies provided the total value reaches the nisab threshold. The commentary explains the permissibility of paying the monetary value instead of the animal itself in zakat, expiations (kaffarah), the tithe on land (ushr), and vowed charity. The reason given is that mixed horses do not definitively generate growth through reproduction because females are kept for breeding, but growth is not guaranteed as with separate males. However, a stronger opinion (rajih) says zakat is wajib because they can reproduce using a borrowed stallion. The option to pay value is traced back to Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, and a weak hadith in Daraqutni also mentions a dinar per horse. The commentary on 'nisaban' clarifies it is a circumstantial qualifier of the value. The permissibility of paying value is justified by the purpose of zakat being to deliver benefit to the poor, which is equally achieved by money.

Key Terms
سائمة (sā'imah): Grazing livestock that feed on natural pasture for most of the year; used here for horses.
نصاب (niṣāb): Minimum threshold of wealth that makes zakat obligatory; here it refers to the value of the horses reaching the nisab.
قيمة (qīmah): Monetary value or price; in this context, paying the cash equivalent instead of the actual item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is permitted only in zakat, not in expiations.
C) It is permitted in zakat, expiations, ushr, and vowed charity.
D) It is permitted only if the nisab condition is waived.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits paying the cash value instead of the animal itself in zakat on mixed grazing horses, with the preponderant view making zakat wajib due to potential reproduction.

Zakat on Horses: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat obligation for horses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding zakat on horses, identify the evidence for each position, and explain which view is the mufta bihi according to later Hanafi authorities.

Core Idea
The Hanafi fatwa on horses is no zakat, despite Abu Hanifa's personal view.

The obligation of zakat on horses is not settled even within the Hanafi school. While Imam Abu Hanifa held that zakat is due on mixed grazing horses, his two closest disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, maintained that no zakat is due on horses at all. This lesson examines their evidence and traces how later Hanafi jurists resolved the dispute, establishing the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa holds that zakat is due on mixed grazing horses. The Sahibayn hold that no zakat is due on horses at all.
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute, meaning both views are within the school.
2
Examine the evidence for each
The Sahibayn rely on the hadith 'No sadaqah on a Muslim's slave or his horse' and the Prophet's exemption. Abu Hanifa relies on a hadith about a dinar per horse (weak) and the practice of early Muslims like Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.
The strength of the hadiths is a key factor in the tarjih.
3
Trace the tarjih among later authorities
Al-Hawi, al-Yanabi', al-Jawahir, al-Khaniyyah, al-Kafi, al-Bazzaziyyah all favor the Sahibayn. Ibn al-Humam prefers Abu Hanifa.
The majority of later authoritative texts adopt the Sahibayn's view as the fatwa.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling for fatwa is that there is no zakat on horses, following the Sahibayn.
This is despite Abu Hanifa's personal view, but his view is not the relied-upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنده

According to him. And according to the two, there is absolutely no zakat on horses, due to the hadith: 'There is no sadaqah due from a Muslim for his slave or his horse,' transmitted by the Sunan, and in a narration: 'I have exempted sadaqah on horses and slaves,' authenticated by al-Tirmidhi and Abu Dawood and others. Al-Hawi preferred their view, and in al-Yanabi', al-Jawahir, al-Khaniyyah, al-Kafi, and al-Bazzaziyyah: the fatwa is upon their view. And Ibn al-Humam preferred his (Abu Hanifa's) view.
(1) In al-Athar of Abu Yusuf (p. 436) and others.
(2) From al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid: 'I saw my father value horses and then pay their sadaqah to 'Umar' narrated by al-Daraqutni in Ghara'ib Malik with a sound chain. See I'la al-Sunan (9:37) and others. And from Jabir: 'For grazing horses, each horse a dinar paid' in Sunan al-Daraqutni (2:125) and Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (4:119), and they said: it is unique to Furk from Ja'far and he is very weak, and those below him are weak. And in Fath Bab al-'Inayah (1:493) a refutation of their statement.
(3) Al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah (1:249).
(4) Al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyyah (4:84). And the author of al-Mawahib (folio 50/b) said: it is the most correct for fatwa.
(5) In Fath al-Qadir (2:139) and also confirmed by the author of al-Ikhtiyar (1:141) and al-Durr al-Muntaqa (1:201) and others.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the obligation of zakat on horses. After discussing Abu Hanifa's view that mixed horses are subject to zakat, the text turns to the companions' position: no zakat on horses at all, absolutely. Their primary evidence is the hadith 'There is no sadaqah due from a Muslim for his slave or his horse,' which is recorded in the major hadith collections. Another narration states that the Prophet exempted sadaqah on horses and slaves. The text then surveys later jurists: al-Hawi (probably al-Hawi al-Qudsi) preferred the companions' view, and many later works like al-Yanabi', al-Jawahir, al-Khaniyyah, al-Kafi, and al-Bazzaziyyah state that the fatwa is upon the companions' view. However, Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir preferred Abu Hanifa's view. The footnotes provide additional sources: a report from al-Sa'ib ibn Yazid that his father valued horses and paid zakat to 'Umar, indicating practice; and a weak hadith from Jabir about a dinar per horse. The author ultimately reports that the relied-upon fatwa is the companions' position, despite Ibn al-Humam's preference for Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
عندهما (ʿindahumā): According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the operative fatwa (mufta bihi) regarding zakat on horses according to most later Hanafi sources cited in this passage?
A) Zakat is due on mixed horses only.
B) Zakat is due on all horses.
C) There is no zakat on horses at all.
D) Zakat is due only on horses that are used for trade.

Key Takeaway
The operative Hanafi fatwa on horses is that no zakat is due, following the Sahibayn, despite Abu Hanifa's personal view.
